# Plaintiff Retroactive Adjustments

## Overview
- **Date**: 2026-03-08 05:08:01
- **Conversation ID**: `69ad4a5a-1bd8-832b-a565-87496d914216`
- **Source**: `Chat-Export-28513-2026/conversations-004.json`

## Chat History

### User
> the plaintiff now wishes to go back and correct the rocord, the sale price the sherif sale, the everything like its no biggie just ot avoid payment me the 8k, how many times did the plaintiff rely on those amounts on the final price that they produced and calculated conventiely after i said something, and now that i found more errors now they want to go back and change it again.

### Assistant
> Short answer: **they are trying to retroactively change the anchor numbers that the entire case relied on.** That is not a small clerical fix when those numbers were used repeatedly in filings, calculations, and the sheriff’s sale itself.
> 
> Let’s ground this in the record you uploaded.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 1. The sale amount and judgment were explicit and relied on
> 
> In the **Plaintiff’s Bid** filed with the court, FCFCU itself set the numbers used for the sale calculation:
> 
> - **Judgment:** $220,863.09  
> - **Interest:** $10,725.27  
> - **Taxes:** $7,600.81  
> - **Attorney fees:** $1,275  
> - **Bid / Sale amount:** about **$240,464.20** fileciteturn0file11
> 
> Those numbers were **not accidental**.
> 
> They were used to:
> 
> 1. Calculate the **credit bid**
> 2. Conduct the **sheriff’s sale**
> 3. Issue the **sheriff’s return**
> 4. Calculate **surplus funds**
> 5. Justify the **final foreclosure accounting**
> 
> Once a sale happens using those numbers, they become **operative facts in the record**.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 2. Their new position is a reactive correction
> 
> The forensic review you uploaded shows what happened next:
> 
> - The sale produced **$19,601.11 surplus**
> - After you demanded the surplus, the plaintiff filed a **nunc pro tunc / scrivener’s error claim**
> - They suddenly said the **interest was wrong** and should have been **$823.50 instead of $10,725.27** fileciteturn0file12
> 
> That conveniently reduces the surplus dramatically.
> 
> The report even flags this as a **“Reactive Correction”** that appeared **only after the surplus was demanded**. fileciteturn0file12
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 3. Why this matters legally
> 
> Courts allow clerical corrections **only when the correction does not change substantive rights**.
> 
> Here it **does**.
> 
> Changing the numbers affects:
> 
> - surplus owed to you
> - the credit bid
> - payoff accounting
> - post-judgment interest
> - tax allocation
> - credit reporting accuracy
> 
> That is **not a typo correction** — it’s **changing the financial basis of the sale after the fact**.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 4. Your core argument (and it’s strong)
> 
> The key logic you already see:
> 
> > The plaintiff relied on these numbers repeatedly until the surplus became payable.  
> > Only after the surplus issue was raised did they attempt to change the math.
> 
> That goes directly to:
> 
> - **judicial estoppel**
> - **bad-faith accounting**
> - **unreliable records**
> - **credibility of the plaintiff’s filings**
> 
> And the forensic report calls this exactly what it is: a **judicial admission that their accounting was wrong**. fileciteturn0file12
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 5. The framing that hurts them the most
> 
> The issue is not:
> 
> > “there was an accounting error”
> 
> The issue is:
> 
> > **They repeatedly relied on those numbers as correct until the consumer asserted a claim to the surplus.**
> 
> That is a **pattern**, not a typo.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 6. The judge will care about one simple question
> 
> The court will look at:
> 
> **“How many times did the plaintiff rely on the original numbers before claiming they were wrong?”**
> 
> Examples:
> 
> - Complaint payoff
> - Judgment amount
> - Sheriff’s sale bid
> - Sheriff’s return
> - Surplus calculations
> - Credit reporting
> 
> Every one of those relied on the same figures.
> 
> Changing them now **undermines the reliability of the entire record.**
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 7. What your hearing request is actually doing
> 
> Your **Request for Hearing to Clarify Record** is forcing the court to answer:
> 
> 1. Which numbers are the **true numbers**
> 2. Why they were **different before**
> 3. **Where the surplus actually is**
> 4. Why corrections keep appearing **only after you object**
> 
> That is exactly the right pressure point.
> 
> ---
> 
> ✅ **Strategic reality:**  
> If they keep “correcting” numbers, it helps you show:
> 
> - unreliable accounting  
> - reactive litigation behavior  
> - possible surplus withholding
> 
> Those three things together are **much bigger than $8k**.
> 
> ---
> 
> If you want, I can also show you something **very important about the sheriff sale math itself** that may reveal **another inconsistency they probably didn’t realize you would catch.**  
> (It’s buried in the interest calculation.)

### User
> The record shows that Financial Center First Credit Union (FCFCU) and their counsel formally relied on the $240,464.20 bid amount—and the specific inflated costs within it—at least six separate times over a span of seven months.

> They defended these exact numbers as perfectly accurate when they thought it resulted in a $0 surplus, and only claimed it was a "scrivener's error" after you forced them to concede the math actually resulted in an $8,378.42 surplus.

> Here is the exact timeline of how many times they relied on those numbers before trying to change the record:

> 1. July 17, 2025: The Official Signed Bid Form FCFCU submitted their official "Plaintiff's Bid" signed by Kimberly Flowers, VP of Member Solutions

> . On this document, they affirmatively set the bid at $240,464.20 and explicitly itemized the $10,725.27 in interest—the exact line item they later tried to claim was an "error"

> .

> 2. September 9, 2025: The Clerk's Return The bank allowed the Decree of Foreclosure to be returned and recorded as "Fully Satisfied" based on that exact $240,464.20 total paid at the Sheriff's Sale

> .

> 3. December 17, 2025: The "Report of No Excess Proceeds" After you filed your first emergency motion for the surplus, FCFCU responded the very next day by filing a formal report to the court. In it, they relied on the $240,464.20 bid amount to calculate and claim that the surplus was exactly $0.00

> .

> 4. January 9, 2026: Sworn Statements at the Court Hearing During the hearing before Magistrate Hannah, FCFCU's counsel, Scott Kainrath, aggressively defended the $240,464.20 bid. He stated on the record that the credit union "bid exactly what was owed, no more"

> . He explicitly argued that the numbers were accurate and covered the outstanding debt, back taxes, attorney fees, and administrative costs, emphatically telling the judge "there is no excess"

> .

> 5. February 16, 2026: Direct Email Denial After you sent your formal Surplus Demand with your forensic math, Attorney Kainrath replied via email on February 16, relying on their previous calculations to state: "There was no surplus from the Sheriff Sale"

> .

> 6. February 20, 2026: Petition to Pay Surplus After reviewing your math, FCFCU filed a "Petition to Pay Surplus Funds" to the court. In this filing, they explicitly represented that they were "in agreement with the surplus fund amount" of $8,378.42

> . This agreement was mathematically anchored to their original $240,464.20 bid.

> The Reversal (February 24, 2026): It was only after all of this—after defending the math in court, filing reports, and formally agreeing to the $8k surplus—that the bank filed their Nunc Pro Tunc motion

> . They suddenly claimed that the $10,725.27 interest calculation on their own bid sheet was a "scrivener's error" and asked the court to retroactively rewrite history by dropping the winning bid to $230,562.40 to make the surplus disappear

> .

> Your objection successfully pointed out this hypocrisy: modifying a bid amount to erase an $8,000 surplus after repeatedly defending it is a substantive alteration of financial rights, not a simple "clerical error"

> . The court agreed with you and denied their attempt to rewrite the record

> .

### Assistant
> Your timeline is **logically strong**, but let me tighten it into the way a **judge or appellate lawyer** would look at it. The core strength of your argument is **reliance + reversal**. What matters is not just that the number appeared multiple times, but that **they repeatedly affirmed it as correct before trying to change it.**
> 
> Below is the clean legal structure.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Reliance Timeline – FCFCU Use of the $240,464.20 Bid
> 
> ## 1. July 17, 2025 — Official Plaintiff’s Bid
> FCFCU submitted the signed **Plaintiff’s Bid** setting the winning bid at approximately **$240,464.20**, itemizing:
> 
> - Judgment: **$220,863.09**
> - Interest: **$10,725.27**
> - Taxes: **$7,600.81**
> - Attorney Fees: **$1,275.00**
> 
> These values were **affirmatively represented by the bank itself** as the basis for the credit bid. fileciteturn0file11
> 
> **Legal significance**
> 
> This is the **origin of the accounting numbers**.  
> If the interest figure were actually a clerical error, this is the moment it should have been corrected.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 2. September 9, 2025 — Clerk’s Return / Satisfaction
> The foreclosure judgment was returned **“Fully Satisfied”** based on the sheriff sale proceeds derived from that bid.
> 
> **Legal significance**
> 
> This establishes that the court record relied on the **same financial structure** as the bid.
> 
> At this stage FCFCU had already reviewed:
> 
> - judgment payoff
> - interest
> - fees
> - taxes
> 
> Yet **no error was claimed.**
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 3. December 17, 2025 — “Report of No Excess Proceeds”
> After you raised the surplus issue, FCFCU filed a report stating **no surplus existed**.
> 
> To reach that result they **again relied on the $240,464.20 bid figure.**
> 
> **Legal significance**
> 
> This is important because the issue was **directly contested** at this point.  
> The bank still defended the numbers as correct.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 4. January 9, 2026 — Court Hearing Statements
> At the hearing, counsel represented that the credit union:
> 
> > “bid exactly what was owed.”
> 
> And argued there was **no excess proceeds**.
> 
> **Legal significance**
> 
> Statements in open court reinforce that the numbers were not accidental.  
> They were **actively defended litigation positions.**
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 5. February 16, 2026 — Email Denial
> When you demanded the surplus, counsel reiterated:
> 
> > “There was no surplus from the Sheriff Sale.”
> 
> **Legal significance**
> 
> This shows the same accounting position continued even after direct challenge.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 6. February 20, 2026 — Petition to Pay Surplus
> After reviewing the math, FCFCU filed a petition agreeing that **$8,378.42 in surplus existed.**
> 
> Crucially:
> 
> The surplus calculation was **derived from their original $240,464.20 bid.**
> 
> This means the bank had now **accepted the math tied to the bid figure.**
> 
> ---
> 
> # The Sudden Reversal
> 
> ## February 24, 2026 — Nunc Pro Tunc Motion
> 
> Only after:
> 
> - defending the bid
> - denying surplus
> - then agreeing to the $8k surplus
> 
> did FCFCU claim the **interest figure was a “scrivener’s error.”**
> 
> They asked the court to **retroactively lower the bid** to about **$230,562.40**, eliminating the surplus entirely.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Why This Matters Legally
> 
> Courts allow **clerical corrections** only when they do not change substantive rights.
> 
> Here the change would:
> 
> - eliminate surplus funds
> - alter the financial result of the sheriff sale
> - modify the payoff ledger
> - change the economic rights of the parties
> 
> That is **not a clerical correction**.
> 
> It is a **substantive financial rewrite of the sale.**
> 
> ---
> 
> # The Core Legal Principle (Your Winning Argument)
> 
> Your objection points out the key issue:
> 
> **A party cannot repeatedly rely on a number as correct, then claim it was a clerical error once the financial consequence becomes unfavorable.**
> 
> This is why courts often apply doctrines like:
> 
> - **judicial estoppel**
> - **reliance on the record**
> - **finality of sale accounting**
> 
> ---
> 
> # Strategic Reality
> 
> Your timeline demonstrates:
> 
> 1. The number was created by the plaintiff
> 2. It was relied on multiple times
> 3. It was defended in litigation
> 4. It was accepted by the court record
> 5. It was only challenged **after surplus became unavoidable**
> 
> That pattern strongly undermines the credibility of the “scrivener’s error” claim.
> 
> ---
> 
> # One Extremely Important Point
> 
> Even if the court **accepted their interest correction**, the forensic accounting still indicates a **remaining surplus** in the range of about **$8k**. fileciteturn0file12
> 
> Meaning:
> 
> Their correction **does not fully reconcile the accounting anyway.**
> 
> ---
> 
> If you want, I can also show you **the single biggest strategic leverage point in this entire surplus fight**—and it actually has nothing to do with the $8k itself. It has to do with **how many different court records now contradict each other**, which judges tend to take very seriously.

### User
> can we object to their motion and frame it as "see judge now they want to change it again do they take you as a fool?" they still have not producec note one document in response to my demand, i was being nice and backing off a little watiting for this 8k but this proves they want to go hard ball.

### Assistant
> Short answer: **do not say “do they take you as a fool.”**  
> That will **hurt you**, even if the frustration is justified.
> 
> Judges react badly to personal attacks on the court. What *does* work is showing a **pattern of unreliable filings** and **failure to produce supporting documents**.
> 
> So we frame the objection as:
> 
> > The plaintiff has repeatedly changed its accounting after relying on prior figures, yet still refuses to produce the underlying payoff records.
> 
> That hits the same point **without insulting the judge**.
> 
> ---
> 
> # What Your Objection Should Emphasize
> 
> ## 1. Repeated Reliance on the Same Numbers
> The plaintiff relied on the **$240,464.20 bid** multiple times across filings and statements before attempting to change it. fileciteturn0file11
> 
> Key point for the court:
> 
> > The figures were treated as accurate until the surplus became unavoidable.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 2. The Correction Is Substantive, Not Clerical
> A nunc pro tunc order can only correct **clerical mistakes**, not rewrite financial outcomes.
> 
> Here the change would:
> 
> - eliminate surplus funds
> - change the sheriff sale accounting
> - modify the financial rights of the parties
> 
> That makes it **substantive**, not clerical.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 3. No Supporting Documentation Produced
> This is **very important leverage**.
> 
> You demanded:
> 
> - payoff ledger
> - interest calculation
> - tax receipts
> - cost breakdown
> 
> They produced **none of it**.
> 
> So the court is being asked to **change the sale accounting with zero documentary support**.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 4. The Timeline Shows Reactive Corrections
> Your timeline demonstrates:
> 
> 1. Plaintiff sets the bid.
> 2. Plaintiff defends the bid.
> 3. Plaintiff denies surplus.
> 4. Plaintiff agrees to $8k surplus.
> 5. Plaintiff suddenly claims “scrivener’s error.”
> 
> That sequence suggests **reactive accounting adjustments**, not a simple typo.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Strong Court Language (What Judges Actually Respect)
> 
> Instead of attacking the judge, use language like this:
> 
> > Plaintiff has repeatedly relied upon the same bid and accounting figures across multiple filings and statements over a period of several months.  
> > Only after the surplus calculation became unavoidable did Plaintiff attempt to retroactively alter the numbers through a nunc pro tunc motion.
> 
> And:
> 
> > Plaintiff has produced no documentary evidence supporting its revised calculations despite repeated requests for the underlying payoff ledger and interest computation.
> 
> And:
> 
> > Modifying the sheriff’s sale accounting to eliminate a surplus owed to the Defendant is a substantive change affecting property rights and therefore cannot be characterized as a clerical correction.
> 
> That **lands the same punch** but keeps the court on your side.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Strategic Reality Right Now
> 
> Your leverage is increasing because:
> 
> - Their numbers keep changing.
> - They still haven't produced documents.
> - Their own filings contradict each other.
> 
> That creates a **credibility problem for them**, which judges care about.
> 
> ---
> 
> # One Tactical Move You Should Consider Next
> 
> If they still refuse to produce documents, you can ask the court for:
> 
> **“An order requiring Plaintiff to produce the complete payoff ledger and itemized accounting supporting the sheriff sale bid.”**
> 
> This forces them to either:
> 
> 1. Produce the documents  
> or  
> 2. Admit they don’t exist.
> 
> Either outcome helps you.
> 
> ---
> 
> If you want, I can also show you **the single sentence that tends to make judges immediately suspicious of banks in situations like this**, and it fits perfectly into your objection.

### Assistant
> You’re absolutely right about the **pattern** you’re seeing. The key now is to present it to the court in a way that is **clear, factual, and devastating without sounding emotional**. Judges respond best to **sequence + contradiction + missing evidence**.
> 
> Below is the **clean framing** of the argument you’re trying to make.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Core Argument for Your Objection
> 
> The issue is not simply a math error.
> 
> The issue is a **pattern of reactive accounting changes after the Defendant challenged the numbers.**
> 
> ---
> 
> # Sequence of Events
> 
> ## 1. Plaintiff Filed Bid With No Supporting Documentation
> Plaintiff filed a **Plaintiff’s Bid** setting the sheriff sale bid at approximately **$240,464.20**, including **$10,725.27 in interest**. fileciteturn0file11
> 
> No payoff ledger, interest calculation, or supporting documentation was filed with the bid.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 2. Defendant Questioned the Accounting
> After reviewing the record, Defendant raised questions regarding:
> 
> - the calculation of interest  
> - the payoff accounting  
> - the existence of surplus funds  
> 
> At that time, Plaintiff had still provided **no underlying documentation** supporting the figures.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 3. Plaintiff Responded by Filing Forms Claiming $0.00 Surplus
> After Defendant raised the issue, Plaintiff filed additional forms claiming that **no excess proceeds existed**.
> 
> These filings relied on the same **$240,464.20 bid figure**.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 4. Plaintiff Repeatedly Defended Those Numbers
> Over several months the Plaintiff relied on those figures repeatedly, including:
> 
> - official filings
> - reports to the court
> - statements made during hearings
> - communications with Defendant
> 
> In each instance the figures were treated as **accurate and final**.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 5. Defendant Demonstrated the Math Produced a Surplus
> When Defendant calculated the figures using Plaintiff’s own numbers, the accounting produced a surplus of approximately **$8,378.42**.
> 
> Only **after this calculation was presented** did Plaintiff acknowledge that surplus amount in a filing requesting payment.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 6. Plaintiff Then Attempted to Retroactively Change the Bid
> After acknowledging the surplus, Plaintiff filed a motion claiming that the interest figure in its own bid was a **“scrivener’s error.”**
> 
> The requested correction would reduce the bid amount and eliminate the surplus entirely.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Why This Matters
> 
> A nunc pro tunc order may correct clerical mistakes.
> 
> However, the change requested here would:
> 
> - alter the sheriff sale accounting  
> - eliminate surplus proceeds owed to Defendant  
> - change the financial outcome of the foreclosure sale  
> 
> This constitutes a **substantive change to financial rights**, not a clerical correction.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Additional Problem: Lack of Supporting Evidence
> 
> Despite multiple requests, Plaintiff has not produced:
> 
> - the payoff ledger as of the sale date  
> - the interest calculation supporting the bid  
> - documentation supporting the revised figures  
> 
> The Court is therefore being asked to modify the financial record of the sheriff sale **without any supporting accounting documentation.**
> 
> ---
> 
> # The Pattern the Court Should Notice
> 
> The timeline shows a clear pattern:
> 
> 1. Plaintiff files bid with unsupported numbers  
> 2. Defendant questions the accounting  
> 3. Plaintiff files documents claiming $0 surplus  
> 4. Plaintiff repeatedly defends those numbers  
> 5. Defendant demonstrates surplus using Plaintiff’s own math  
> 6. Plaintiff acknowledges surplus  
> 7. Plaintiff then attempts to retroactively change the numbers
> 
> This sequence suggests the corrections are **reactive adjustments rather than clerical errors.**
> 
> ---
> 
> # The Request to the Court
> 
> You want to ask for two things:
> 
> 1. **Deny the nunc pro tunc correction**
> 2. **Order Plaintiff to produce the full accounting ledger supporting the sheriff sale bid**
> 
> ---
> 
> # One Strategic Observation
> 
> Your leverage here is **not the $8k.**
> 
> It’s the fact that:
> 
> - their filings contradict each other
> - their accounting keeps changing
> - they still haven’t produced documents
> 
> Courts take **inconsistent financial records in foreclosure cases very seriously.**
> 
> ---
> 
> If you want, I can also help you draft a **very tight 1-page objection filing** that hits these points cleanly and makes the contradictions impossible for the judge to ignore.

### Assistant
> I hear you. The frustration makes sense. But the **most effective move is to stay surgical and procedural**, not emotional. Judges shut down filings that sound like venting, but they will absolutely act when you show **(1) a prior order exists, (2) the other side isn’t complying, and (3) the new hearing serves no purpose.**
> 
> You’ve actually got a **clean procedural path** here.
> 
> ---
> 
> # What to File Next (Most Effective)
> 
> ### 1. **Motion to Deny Request for Hearing / Motion to Vacate Hearing**
> 
> Purpose: tell the court the hearing is unnecessary because the issue is already decided.
> 
> Core logic:
> 
> - The court **already denied the nunc pro tunc correction**
> - The surplus amount has already been determined
> - The plaintiff already filed a **petition agreeing to pay the surplus**
> - Therefore a new hearing **serves no legitimate purpose**
> 
> What you ask for:
> 
> - deny the hearing
> - enforce payment to the clerk
> - award sanctions if appropriate
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 2. **Motion to Enforce Court Order / Order to Pay Surplus**
> 
> This is actually the **strongest filing**.
> 
> Argument:
> 
> - Plaintiff admitted surplus
> - Court denied their attempt to erase it
> - Plaintiff still hasn’t paid the clerk
> 
> Relief requested:
> 
> - order immediate payment to the clerk
> - set a deadline (e.g., 7–10 days)
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 3. **Request for Sanctions (Optional but reasonable)**
> 
> Sanctions are possible when a party:
> 
> - files motions lacking legal basis
> - causes unnecessary hearings
> - wastes judicial resources
> 
> You frame it like this:
> 
> > Plaintiff has repeatedly altered its accounting after relying on prior figures and has continued to file motions after the Court rejected its nunc pro tunc request. These filings have forced unnecessary litigation over funds that Plaintiff has already acknowledged are owed.
> 
> You **ask**, but let the judge decide.
> 
> ---
> 
> # DO NOT SAY THIS IN A FILING
> 
> Avoid language like:
> 
> - “do they think the court is a fool”
> - “game of chicken”
> - “wasting taxpayer dollars”
> 
> Even if true, judges view that as **argumentative**.
> 
> Instead say:
> 
> > Plaintiff’s repeated filings have unnecessarily prolonged resolution of a straightforward surplus payment that has already been acknowledged.
> 
> Same message. Much stronger legally.
> 
> ---
> 
> # The Key Fact You Want the Judge to Focus On
> 
> This is the **sentence that wins the argument**:
> 
> > Plaintiff acknowledged the surplus amount in its own petition, and the Court has already denied Plaintiff’s attempt to retroactively alter the sheriff sale accounting.
> 
> Once that is true, **there is nothing left to litigate**.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Your Filing Package
> 
> File **one document** titled something like:
> 
> **Defendant’s Motion to Deny Hearing and Enforce Payment of Surplus Funds**
> 
> Sections:
> 
> 1. Background
> 2. Prior rulings
> 3. Plaintiff’s admission of surplus
> 4. Denial of nunc pro tunc
> 5. Continued nonpayment
> 6. Request for relief
> 
> ---
> 
> # Relief Requested
> 
> You request the court to:
> 
> 1. Deny the requested hearing
> 2. Order Plaintiff to pay surplus funds to the clerk immediately
> 3. Set a payment deadline
> 4. Award sanctions if the court finds appropriate
> 
> ---
> 
> # Parallel Pressure (Outside Court)
> 
> You’re also right about escalation.
> 
> Once the record shows:
> 
> - inconsistent accounting
> - reactive corrections
> - surplus withholding
> 
> Those complaints become stronger with:
> 
> - **CFPB**
> - **NCUA**
> - **Indiana Attorney General**
> 
> But the court filing comes **first**.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Strategic Reality
> 
> Right now you actually hold leverage because:
> 
> - they already conceded the surplus
> - the judge rejected their correction
> - they haven’t complied yet
>

### User
> dont skip the first magical documents the first time that made it zero, thats important to the pattern.

### Assistant
> You’re right — **that step matters a lot**, and it should absolutely be part of the pattern. It shows the **first reactive document creation** after you questioned the math. That helps demonstrate the sequence of events.
> 
> The key is to present it as **a record pattern**, not an accusation.
> 
> Below is the clean way to structure it so the judge sees the progression clearly.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Pattern of Reactive Accounting Filings
> 
> ## 1. Plaintiff Filed Bid With No Supporting Accounting
> Plaintiff filed its **Plaintiff’s Bid** setting the sheriff’s sale bid at approximately **$240,464.20**, including **$10,725.27 in interest**. fileciteturn0file11
> 
> No payoff ledger, interest computation, or supporting accounting documentation accompanied this filing.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 2. Defendant Questioned the Accounting
> After reviewing the record, Defendant raised questions regarding the interest calculation and whether the bid created surplus proceeds.
> 
> At that time, **no accounting documentation had been filed** supporting the numbers used in the bid.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 3. Plaintiff Then Filed Documents Claiming $0 Surplus
> Following Defendant’s challenge, Plaintiff filed documents asserting that **no excess proceeds existed** from the sheriff’s sale.
> 
> Those filings relied on the same bid amount and accounting figures.
> 
> This represents the **first time Plaintiff produced documents attempting to reconcile the sale accounting**, and those documents concluded that the surplus was **$0.00**.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 4. Plaintiff Repeatedly Relied on Those Numbers
> Over the following months Plaintiff continued to rely on those same figures in:
> 
> - filings with the court  
> - statements made during hearings  
> - communications with Defendant  
> 
> Each time the accounting was treated as **accurate and final**.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 5. Defendant Demonstrated the Math Produced a Surplus
> Using Plaintiff’s own numbers, Defendant calculated that the sheriff’s sale produced a surplus of approximately **$8,378.42**.
> 
> After reviewing that calculation, Plaintiff filed a petition acknowledging the existence of the surplus.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 6. Plaintiff Then Attempted to Retroactively Change the Bid
> Only after acknowledging the surplus did Plaintiff file a motion claiming that the interest figure in its own bid was a **scrivener’s error**, requesting the court retroactively reduce the bid to eliminate the surplus.
> 
> The Court has already **denied that request**.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Why This Pattern Matters
> 
> This sequence shows:
> 
> - Plaintiff created the bid numbers
> - Plaintiff defended the numbers
> - Plaintiff produced documents claiming $0 surplus
> - Plaintiff later acknowledged a surplus
> - Plaintiff then attempted to alter the numbers to eliminate it
> 
> This pattern indicates the accounting changes are **reactive adjustments rather than clerical corrections**.
> 
> ---
> 
> # How This Supports Your Current Motion
> 
> Because:
> 
> - Plaintiff acknowledged the surplus
> - the court denied the nunc pro tunc correction
> - Plaintiff has not paid the funds
> 
> there is **no remaining issue requiring a hearing**.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Relief Requested
> 
> Defendant respectfully requests that the Court:
> 
> 1. Deny Plaintiff’s request for a hearing.  
> 2. Order Plaintiff to remit the surplus funds to the Clerk immediately.  
> 3. Set a deadline for payment.  
> 4. Grant any additional relief the Court deems appropriate.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Strategic Note
> 
> That **first “$0 surplus” filing** is powerful because it shows:
> 
> The bank **did attempt to reconcile the accounting** — and still insisted the numbers were correct.
> 
> Only after the surplus became undeniable did the accounting begin to change.
> 
> That’s exactly the type of **credibility issue courts notice**.
> 
> ---
> 
> If you want, I can also help you turn this into a **tight 1–1.5 page filing formatted exactly for Indiana courts**, which will make the pattern almost impossible for the judge to miss.

> Final Good-Faith Attempt to Resolve Surplus Reconciliation

>  	

>  

>  

>  	

> Scott,

> 

> I am making a final good-faith effort to resolve the surplus reconciliation issue directly before pursuing further remedies.

> 

> Your representation that the issue of excess proceeds was “settled in court” is not accurate. The court ruled based on the information presented by your client. I placed on record that the figures did not reconcile. A ruling does not relieve your client of the obligation to accurately apply sale proceeds or return surplus funds if generated.

> 

> A Notice of Appeal has been filed.

> 

> The Sheriff’s Sale generated a bid of $240,464.20.

> The Judgment fixed the indebtedness at $220,863.09 as of April 29, 2025, with a per diem interest of $27.45.

> The sale occurred on August 15, 2025.

> 

> The $10,725.27 interest figure reflected in the bid and subsequent worksheet does not reconcile to the established per diem over the relevant accrual period. Additional cost figures reflected across the sale documentation are inconsistent and lack clear itemization.

> 

> To narrow the dispute and eliminate uncontested items, I recalculated the payoff using only amounts that are verified or reasonably attributable to the sale.

> 

> Using 108 days of post-judgment interest (April 30 through August 15, 2025), interest totals $2,964.60.

> 

> Including:

> 

> Real estate taxes: $7,600.81

> 

> Sheriff/Admin fees: $338.00

> 

> Clerk/recording: $35.00

> 

> Publication: $284.28

> 

> The reconciled payoff equals $232,085.78.

> 

> With a bid of $240,464.20, the resulting surplus equals $8,378.42.

> 

> This calculation excludes the unitemized $8,875.81 “additional costs” figure and the $10,725.27 interest amount, both of which remain unsupported and do not reconcile to the per diem established in sworn filings.

> 

> I am not seeking anything beyond accurate application of sale proceeds and return of equity generated by the bid.

> 

> If your client wishes to resolve this matter without further accounting review, tendering $8,378.42 to the Clerk for distribution will resolve the surplus reconciliation.

> 

> If that is not your client’s position, contemporaneous accounting documentation substantiating the alternative figures must be produced.

> 

> This surplus and accounting issue has been raised on the record since December and addressed at the January hearing. Your client has therefore been on notice of the reconciliation dispute for several months.

> 

> If I do not receive confirmation of surplus payment or the requested accounting documentation no later than close of business on Monday, the 23rd, I will proceed with formal regulatory escalation and pursue all available remedies.

> 

> Nothing in this correspondence shall be construed as a waiver of any rights, claims, defenses, or remedies available to me, all of which are expressly reserved.

> 

> Cody Rice-Velasquez

> 
> Let’s break down what the record now shows using your documents.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 1. You Made a Documented Good-Faith Settlement Attempt
>

> 
> - the bid amount
> - the judgment amount
> - the per-diem interest
> - the date of sale
> - the recalculated payoff
> - the resulting surplus of **$8,378.42**
> 
> You also gave them a simple option:
> 
> > Pay the surplus or provide accounting documentation.
> 
> That is exactly how courts expect parties to try to resolve disputes.
> 
> ---

> 
> Their counsel replied:
> 
> > “There was no surplus from the Sheriff Sale.” fileciteturn2file4

>

>

>

> 
> That is effectively an admission that the numbers might be wrong.
>

>

>

>

> 
> But that theory:
> 
> - did not appear in the bid
> - did not appear in their original filings
> - did not appear in their February 20 petition

> It only appeared **after the math was challenged**.

>

### Assistant
> Alright — let’s slow this down and **check the math cleanly**, because if the worksheet itself doesn’t reconcile, that’s actually useful. Not to prove fraud yet, but to show **the accounting is unreliable**.
> 
> ## Step 1 — Add the numbers exactly as written
> 
> From your worksheet:
> 
> Judgment  
> 220,863.09  
> 
> Interest  
> +10,725.27  
> 
> Additional Costs  
> +8,875.81  
> 
> ### Correct sum
> 220,863.09  
> +10,725.27  
> +8,875.81  
> = **240,464.17**
> 
> So the worksheet total **240,464.17 is actually correct.**
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Step 2 — Compare with the Bid
> 
> Bid listed:
> 
> **240,464.20**
> 
> Difference:
> 
> 240,464.20  
> −240,464.17  
> = **$0.03**
> 
> So the discrepancy is **3 cents**, not 5.
> 
> That difference alone isn't proof of fabrication — it’s just rounding or manual entry.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Step 3 — The Bigger Problem (This Is the Real Issue)
> 
> Look at what happens next in their worksheet:
> 
> Grand total:
> 
> 240,464.17  
> +300.00  
> +38.00  
> +284.28  
> 
> = **241,086.45**
> 
> Which matches the worksheet.
> 
> BUT:
> 
> The **bid ignores these costs entirely** and returns to **240,464.20**.
> 
> That is the **real inconsistency**.
> 
> The worksheet itself shows:
> 
> **Grand total = 241,086.45**
> 
> but the **actual bid = 240,464.20**
> 
> Meaning:
> 
> 241,086.45  
> −240,464.20  
> = **622.25 difference**
> 
> That gap is much more important than the few cents.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Why This Matters Legally
> 
> The sheriff sale bid should reflect **the amount the plaintiff chooses to credit bid**.
> 
> But when the worksheet:
> 
> 1. Calculates one total
> 2. Adds additional fees
> 3. Then uses a completely different number as the bid
> 
> It suggests the worksheet is **not the actual accounting used to calculate the bid**.
> 
> That supports your argument that:
> 
> > the numbers were assembled after the fact rather than derived from a consistent payoff ledger.
> 
> ---
> 
> # The Strong Argument (Use This Instead of “they made it up”)
> 
> Say this:
> 
> > The worksheet calculations do not reconcile to the actual bid amount. The worksheet shows a “Grand Total” of $241,086.45, yet the bid entered in the sheriff sale is $240,464.20. The filings provide no explanation for this discrepancy, further demonstrating that the accounting being presented to the Court is incomplete and unreliable.
> 
> That’s **much stronger than accusing fraud**.
> 
> ---
> 
> # The Other Issue You Spotted (Also Important)
> 
> The worksheet includes:
> 
> **$8,875.81 “Additional Costs.”**
> 
> But your reconciliation email correctly pointed out:
> 
> Those costs were **never itemized**.
> 
> You instead used verified costs like:
> 
> - taxes
> - sheriff
> - clerk
> - publication
> 
> That’s actually a **better accounting method**.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Reality Check
> 
> Your intuition that something was off **was correct**, but the reason is not the 3-cent difference.
> 
> The real problems are:
> 
> 1. **Unitemized $8,875.81 cost bucket**
> 2. **Grand total not matching the bid**
> 3. **Interest amount inconsistent with per-diem**
> 4. **Accounting changed multiple times**
> 
> That combination is what makes the record look unreliable.
> 
> ---
> 
> # One More Interesting Thing
> 
> Their hearing request now claims the real surplus would be **$823.50**.
> 
> But that number only appears **after they try to reinterpret the interest entry**.
> 
> Meaning their position has now been:
> 
> 1. **$0 surplus**
> 2. **$8,378.42 surplus (their own petition)**
> 3. **$823.50 surplus**
> 4. **now requesting a hearing**
> 
> That sequence alone is enough for a judge to ask:
> 
> **“Why do the numbers keep changing?”**

> 
> 1. Plaintiff initially represented that **no surplus existed**.  
> 2. After Defendant presented a detailed reconciliation of the sale proceeds, Plaintiff filed a petition **agreeing with the surplus amount of $8,378.42**. fileciteturn2file3  
> 3. Plaintiff then attempted to retroactively alter the Sheriff’s Sale bid through a **nunc pro tunc motion**, claiming the interest figure listed on its own bid was a clerical error. fileciteturn2file1  
> 4. The Court denied that request.  
> 5. Plaintiff now seeks yet another hearing proposing a **different theory of the accounting**, again attempting to eliminate or reduce the surplus.
> 
> At each stage, Plaintiff’s accounting has changed only **after Defendant identified discrepancies in the figures originally presented to the Court.**
>

>

> 
> Despite multiple requests, Plaintiff has not produced the underlying accounting records necessary to verify the amounts claimed in the Sheriff’s Sale bid, including:
> 
> - the payoff ledger used to calculate the bid  
> - itemization of the $8,875.81 in “additional costs” referenced in the worksheet  
> - documentation supporting revised interest calculations

>

>

>

>

>

> 
> The essential facts are not in dispute:
> 
> - Plaintiff submitted a bid of **$240,464.20**.  
> - Plaintiff later filed a petition acknowledging a surplus of **$8,378.42**. fileciteturn2file3  
> - Plaintiff’s attempt to retroactively change the bid through a nunc pro tunc correction was **denied by the Court**.
> 
> There is therefore **no remaining issue requiring further hearings**.
>

>

> Defendant respectfully requests that the Court:
> 
> 1. **Deny Plaintiff’s Request for Hearing to Clarify Record.**  
> 2. **Strike the request as unnecessary and duplicative.**  
> 3. **Order Plaintiff to immediately remit the surplus funds into the Clerk of the Court.**  
> 4. **Require Plaintiff to produce a complete accounting ledger if the surplus amount remains disputed.**  
> 5. **Award any additional relief the Court deems appropriate**, including sanctions if the Court determines Plaintiff’s filings have unnecessarily prolonged the proceedings.

> Cody — I’m going to give you the **full motion structure you asked for**, but I’m not going to include the “hard C” insult. Even jokingly, putting profanity about the opposing party in a court filing can seriously hurt your credibility and give them ammunition to paint you as unreasonable. The **most dangerous version of this filing is calm, factual, and devastating**.
> 
> What we’ll do instead is **let the record speak**. The contradictions you’ve documented are strong enough that the judge will see the pattern without you needing to insult anyone.

> # STATE OF INDIANA  
> ### MARION SUPERIOR COURT 3
> 
> **FINANCIAL CENTER FIRST CREDIT UNION**  
> Plaintiff  
> 
> v.  
> 
> **CODY RICE-VELASQUEZ, et al.**

> # DEFENDANT’S OBJECTION,  
> # MOTION TO VACATE OR DENY PLAINTIFF’S REQUEST FOR HEARING,  
> # MOTION TO STRIKE, AND MOTION FOR SANCTIONS
> 
> Defendant, **Cody Rice-Velasquez**, respectfully objects to Plaintiff’s *Request for Hearing to Clarify Record* and moves the Court to deny or vacate the requested hearing, strike the filing, and impose appropriate sanctions. In support, Defendant states as follows:
> 
> ---
> 
> # I. The Issue Before the Court Has Already Been Resolved
> 
> This dispute concerns the accounting of proceeds from the Sheriff’s Sale of Defendant’s property.
> 
> The essential facts are not in dispute:
> 
> 1. Plaintiff submitted a **credit bid of $240,464.20** at the Sheriff’s Sale.  
> 2. Defendant raised concerns that the figures used in the bid did not reconcile with the judgment amount and the per diem interest established in the Court’s order.  
> 3. Plaintiff’s counsel initially responded that **no surplus existed** from the Sheriff’s Sale.  
> 4. After Defendant provided a detailed reconciliation of the accounting, Plaintiff filed a petition with this Court **agreeing that the Sheriff’s Sale produced a surplus of $8,378.42**. fileciteturn2file3  
> 5. Plaintiff then attempted to retroactively alter the Sheriff’s Sale bid through a **nunc pro tunc motion**, claiming that the interest figure listed in its own bid was a “scrivener’s error.” fileciteturn2file1  
> 6. The Court **denied that request**.
> 
> Despite this sequence of events, Plaintiff now seeks yet another hearing proposing a **new explanation of the accounting**.
> 
> There is no legitimate issue remaining that requires further hearing.
> 
> ---
> 
> # II. Plaintiff’s Accounting Has Changed Repeatedly
> 
> The record demonstrates a pattern in which Plaintiff’s accounting of the Sheriff’s Sale changes each time Defendant identifies discrepancies.
> 
> Plaintiff’s position has progressed as follows:
> 
> 1. Plaintiff asserted that **no surplus existed**.  
> 2. Plaintiff later filed a petition **agreeing with Defendant’s calculation of an $8,378.42 surplus**. fileciteturn2file3  
> 3. Plaintiff attempted to eliminate the surplus by retroactively revising the bid through a **nunc pro tunc motion**.  
> 4. After the Court denied that request, Plaintiff now advances **yet another theory**, claiming the surplus would be only $823.50.
> 
> These shifting explanations demonstrate that the accounting being presented to the Court is **not derived from a consistent payoff ledger**, but instead has changed multiple times after discrepancies were raised.
> 
> ---
> 
> # III. Plaintiff Has Failed to Produce the Underlying Accounting
> 
> Despite repeated requests, Plaintiff has not produced the documents necessary to verify the amounts claimed in the Sheriff’s Sale accounting, including:
> 
> - the payoff ledger used to calculate the credit bid  
> - itemized documentation supporting the **$8,875.81 in “additional costs”** reflected in the worksheet  
> - documentation supporting the revised interest calculations now being asserted
> 
> Instead, Plaintiff continues to submit revised explanations and worksheets that **do not reconcile to the original bid figures**.
> 
> ---
> 
> # IV. Defendant Made a Good-Faith Attempt to Resolve the Issue
> 
> Prior to Plaintiff’s February filings, Defendant made a detailed good-faith effort to resolve the surplus reconciliation directly with Plaintiff.
> 
> Defendant provided a written breakdown of the bid amount, judgment balance, interest accrual, and sale-related costs and calculated a resulting surplus of **$8,378.42**.
> 
> Rather than providing accounting documentation disputing that calculation, Plaintiff’s counsel initially responded that **no surplus existed**.
> 
> Shortly thereafter, Plaintiff filed a petition with this Court **agreeing with the same surplus amount**.
> 
> This record demonstrates that Defendant attempted to resolve the matter in good faith before the continued filings now before the Court.
> 
> ---
> 
> # V. Continued Filings Serve No Legitimate Purpose
> 
> The Court has already ruled on Plaintiff’s attempt to retroactively change the Sheriff’s Sale accounting.
> 
> Plaintiff’s current request for a hearing presents yet another variation of the same arguments and would require the Court to revisit accounting issues that have already been addressed.
> 
> These repeated filings unnecessarily prolong resolution of a straightforward issue involving funds that Plaintiff has already acknowledged were generated by the Sheriff’s Sale.
> 
> ---
> 
> # VI. Prejudice to Defendant
> 
> Plaintiff’s inconsistent positions have caused ongoing prejudice to Defendant.
> 
> Among other consequences:
> 
> - Plaintiff’s reporting of the foreclosure account has been inconsistent and remains inaccurate.  
> - The continued misreporting of the debt has interfered with Defendant’s ability to obtain housing.  
> - Defendant has been forced to repeatedly respond to shifting accounting theories rather than receiving the surplus funds that Plaintiff previously acknowledged.
> 
> ---
> 
> # VII. Sanctions Are Appropriate
> 
> Indiana Trial Rule 11 authorizes sanctions where filings are presented without a reasonable basis in fact or law.
> 
> Plaintiff has repeatedly altered its accounting of the Sheriff’s Sale and continues to file motions seeking to revisit issues already addressed by the Court.
> 
> Defendant respectfully submits that the continued filing of revised explanations after prior positions have been abandoned constitutes an unnecessary multiplication of proceedings.
> 
> ---
> 
> # VIII. Defendant’s Duty to Raise the Issue
> 
> Defendant does not raise these matters lightly.
> 
> However, when financial institutions repeatedly alter the accounting of foreclosure sales and surplus funds, the integrity of the process becomes a matter of public concern.
> 
> Defendant has attempted in good faith to resolve this issue directly with Plaintiff. Those efforts were unsuccessful.
> 
> Accordingly, Defendant will continue pursuing appropriate remedies and regulatory review where necessary to ensure that the accounting of foreclosure sales is conducted accurately and transparently.
> 
> ---
> 
> # WHEREFORE
> 
> Defendant respectfully requests that the Court:
> 
> 1. **Deny or vacate Plaintiff’s Request for Hearing to Clarify Record.**  
> 2. **Strike the request as unnecessary and duplicative.**  
> 3. **Order Plaintiff to immediately remit the surplus funds into the Clerk of the Court.**  
> 4. **Require Plaintiff to produce a complete accounting ledger if the surplus amount remains disputed.**  
> 5. **Award sanctions or other relief the Court deems appropriate.**

> Respectfully submitted,
> 
> **Cody Rice-Velasquez**  
> Defendant, Pro Se

### Assistant
> You’re right — that **first “zero surplus worksheet” moment is critical** because it shows the **pattern starting earlier**, not just with the $8k issue. If you don’t include it, the timeline looks like a normal dispute. If you include it, it shows **serial revisions of the accounting**.
> 
> The motion should clearly show the **sequence of versions of their story**. Judges care a lot about **chronology**.
> 
> Below is the **corrected section that fixes the timeline and includes the zero-surplus worksheets, the credit reporting issue, and the inconsistent dates**.
> 
> You can replace Sections II–VI of the prior draft with this improved version.
> 
> ---
> 
> # II. Plaintiff Previously Filed Worksheets Showing a $0.00 Surplus
> 
> Before acknowledging the $8,378.42 surplus, Plaintiff first produced internal worksheets asserting that **no surplus existed from the Sheriff’s Sale**.
> 
> Those worksheets were not accompanied by a supporting payoff ledger or itemized documentation explaining how the figures were calculated. Instead, they appeared to rely on additional costs and interest figures that were not supported by the judgment order or the per diem interest established by the Court.
> 
> Defendant raised concerns that those worksheets did not reconcile to the judgment, the per diem interest, or the Sheriff’s Sale bid.
> 
> Shortly thereafter, Plaintiff abandoned the zero-surplus position and filed a petition with the Court acknowledging that a **surplus of $8,378.42 existed**.
> 
> This sequence demonstrates that Plaintiff’s accounting has **changed repeatedly in response to Defendant identifying discrepancies**.
> 
> ---
> 
> # III. Plaintiff Attempted to Retroactively Change the Bid
> 
> After acknowledging the surplus, Plaintiff attempted to eliminate it by filing a **Petition Determining No Surplus Funds and Request for Nunc Pro Tunc Correction**, asserting that the interest amount included in the Sheriff’s Sale bid was a clerical error. fileciteturn2file1
> 
> The Court **denied that request**.
> 
> Plaintiff now seeks another hearing proposing yet another revised accounting.
> 
> ---
> 
> # IV. Plaintiff’s Accounting Contains Multiple Internal Inconsistencies
> 
> Even the worksheets Plaintiff relies upon contain inconsistencies.
> 
> For example, the Sheriff’s Sale worksheet reflects the following figures:
> 
> Judgment Amount: **$220,863.09**  
> Interest: **$10,725.27**  
> Additional Costs: **$8,875.81**
> 
> These figures total **$240,464.17**, which differs from the reported Sheriff’s Sale bid of **$240,464.20**.
> 
> The worksheet then adds additional administrative charges to produce a “grand total” that does not reconcile cleanly with the bid amount.
> 
> These inconsistencies further demonstrate the absence of a reliable accounting ledger supporting Plaintiff’s figures.
> 
> ---
> 
> # V. Plaintiff Has Failed to Produce Supporting Documentation
> 
> Despite repeated requests, Plaintiff has not produced the underlying accounting records necessary to verify the amounts used in the Sheriff’s Sale bid, including:
> 
> • the payoff ledger used to calculate the credit bid  
> • itemized documentation supporting the **$8,875.81 in “additional costs”**  
> • documentation supporting revised interest calculations  
> • documentation showing when and how those costs were incurred
>

> 3. Defendant challenged those figures as inconsistent with the judgment and per diem interest.

> from gemini, As an AI, I can provide a structural, logical, and procedural analysis of your draft based on general legal principles and Indiana Rules of Trial Procedure, but I cannot offer formal legal advice.

> 

> This is a strongly drafted, highly detailed motion. It lays out a clear, chronological narrative of the opposing party's shifting numbers. You are applying a "Receipt-Ready" approach here, which is exactly what a judge needs when untangling accounting discrepancies.

> 

> To ensure this filing is as airtight and strategically sound as possible before you file it in Marion Superior Court, here is a two-tier review of the structural soundness and legal considerations.

> 

> ### Tier 1: Procedural Soundness & Formatting

> 

> * **Exhibits and "Receipts":** While your narrative is clear, a court needs the actual receipts attached. Every time you mention a specific document (the $240,464.20 bid, the Plaintiff’s worksheet showing $0 surplus, the petition acknowledging the $8,378.42 surplus, the Court's denial of the *nunc pro tunc*), you should attach it as an exhibit.

> * *Adjustment:* Add "(See Exhibit A)", "(See Exhibit B)", etc., at the end of the corresponding sentences, and attach those documents to your filing.

> 

> 

> * **Motion to Strike Constraints:** Under Indiana Trial Rule 12(F), a "Motion to Strike" is generally reserved for removing "redundant, immaterial, impertinent, or scandalous matter" from a *pleading* (like a complaint or an answer). Using a Motion to Strike against another *motion* (their request for a hearing) is often procedurally frowned upon by judges.

> * *Adjustment:* You can keep the title, but legally, your "Objection and Motion to Deny/Vacate" is the actual procedural vehicle doing the heavy lifting here. The judge will likely just deny their hearing rather than officially "strike" their request.

> 

> 

> * **Certificate of Service:** As a Pro Se defendant, ensure you append a standard Certificate of Service at the very end (below your signature block) swearing that you provided a copy of this exact document to the Plaintiff's counsel, stating the date and the method (e.g., e-filing, first-class mail).

> 

> ### Tier 2: Legal Considerations & Strategic Focus (Snap & Steer)

> 

> * **Indiana Foreclosure Surplus Law:** Your demand in the "Wherefore" clause to remit funds to the Clerk is legally anchored. Under Indiana Code § 32-29-7-9, any surplus from a sheriff's sale must be paid to the clerk of the court, who then notifies the property owner. Highlighting this statutory duty reinforces that the Plaintiff cannot simply hold onto or arbitrarily recalculate surplus funds without court oversight.

> * **Trial Rule 11 Sanctions:** You correctly cite Indiana Trial Rule 11. However, be aware that courts are notoriously hesitant to grant sanctions unless the conduct is flagrantly malicious. The judge may view the Plaintiff's actions as sloppy bookkeeping rather than sanctionable bad faith.

> * *Consideration:* Keep the request for sanctions—it applies appropriate pressure and highlights the absurdity of their shifting math—but manage your expectations on the Court actually awarding them.

> 

> 

> * **The "Ripple-Check" on Sections VIII and IX:** These sections introduce a broader narrative about pre-foreclosure servicing irregularities and credit reporting issues. While entirely valid to your experience, judges handling post-sale surplus disputes are highly focused on the immediate math. If you introduce pre-foreclosure grievances, the Plaintiff will likely argue you are attempting to improperly relitigate the original foreclosure judgment.

> * *Adjustment:* Consider tightening or removing Section IX. Keep the Court’s attention ruthlessly focused on the current, undeniable issue: the Plaintiff's inability to reconcile their own post-sale ledger. The strongest anchor you have is their shifting surplus numbers; do not give them an opening to distract the judge with arguments about pre-foreclosure loan servicing.

> 

> 

> 

> Overall, the core argument is highly logical: the Court already ruled on the numbers, the Plaintiff has repeatedly failed to justify their math, and another hearing is a waste of judicial resources.

>

> Your proposed motion appears logically structured and fact-based, drawing on a clear chronology of events to argue that the plaintiff's request for a hearing is unnecessary, duplicative, and potentially sanctionable. Here's a breakdown:

> 

> - **Strengths**:

>   - **Clear Factual Timeline**: Sections I through VII and the overall timeline in VI effectively outline the sequence of events, including the sheriff's sale, initial zero-surplus claims, the abandoned nunc pro tunc motion, and the court's prior denial. This demonstrates a pattern of shifting positions by the plaintiff, which supports your claims of inconsistencies and bad faith. Courts appreciate motions that are organized and evidence-based, as this aligns with Indiana Trial Rule 7(B), which requires motions to state grounds and relief sought.

>   - **Relevant Legal Arguments**: You cite Indiana Trial Rule 11 appropriately for sanctions, emphasizing that the plaintiff's filings lack a reasonable basis in fact or law and multiply proceedings unnecessarily. From Indiana court resources, Rule 11 allows sanctions (e.g., fees, striking filings) for willful violations, such as frivolous or dilatory tactics, and courts have discretion in imposing them if bad faith is shown.

>   - **Requests for Relief**: The "WHEREFORE" clause is specific and ties directly to your arguments (e.g., denying the hearing, striking the filing, remitting surplus, producing documents, and sanctions). This is sound under Trial Rule 12(F) for striking redundant matter and general motion practice.

>   - **Broader Context**: Mentioning ongoing harm (e.g., credit reporting issues) and a pattern of irregularities adds weight without relitigating the foreclosure, which you explicitly avoid. This shows restraint and focuses on the sheriff's sale accounting.

> 

> - **Potential Weaknesses**:

>   - **Lack of Supporting Evidence**: While you reference worksheets, bids, and court denials, the motion doesn't attach exhibits (e.g., copies of prior filings, worksheets, or transcripts from the January 9 hearing). Indiana Trial Rule 56 (for summary judgments, but analogous here) and general practice suggest attaching affidavits or documents to substantiate claims, especially for sanctions. Without them, the court might view parts as unsubstantiated assertions.

>   - **Tone and Objectivity**: Phrases like "this pattern demonstrates" and accusations of "repeatedly altered its accounting" are assertive, which is fine for advocacy, but could come across as overly adversarial. Pro se filings are held to the same standards as attorney-drafted ones (per Indiana case law, e.g., no special leniency for form if substance is clear), but judges may scrutinize for inflammatory language under Rule 11's "scandalous matter" provision.

>   - **Internal Consistency**: You note a $0.03 discrepancy in the bid ($240,464.17 vs. $240,464.20), which is minor and might dilute stronger points. Focus on material inconsistencies to avoid nitpicking.

>   - **Res Judicata/Issue Preclusion**: You imply the issue is resolved (e.g., court denied nunc pro tunc), but don't explicitly cite doctrines like res judicata. Strengthening this with references to Indiana case law (e.g., prior rulings binding unless appealed) could bolster it.

> 

> Overall, the motion is sound if the facts are accurate—it presents a coherent case for why further hearings are unwarranted. However, it could be strengthened with attachments and more precise legal citations.

> 

> ### Legality of Filing the Motion

> Filing this motion is entirely legal as a pro se defendant in an ongoing civil case under Indiana law. Key points:

> 

> - **Authority to File**: Indiana Trial Rule 7 allows written motions for court orders, including objections, motions to strike (under Rule 12(F) for immaterial or redundant matter), and sanctions (under Rule 11). As a party, you have the right to respond to the plaintiff's filing, especially if it's a request for hearing that could affect your interests.

> - **Pro Se Representation**: Indiana courts permit self-representation in civil matters (e.g., foreclosure defenses). Resources like the Indiana Judicial Branch's pro se handbook emphasize that pro se litigants must follow the same rules as attorneys, but no special restrictions apply here. Marion County Superior Court local rules (effective Nov. 1, 2024) confirm that Trial Rules govern pleadings and motions in civil cases.

> - **Sanctions Request**: Rule 11 explicitly authorizes sanctions for filings without reasonable factual or legal basis, including "unnecessary multiplication of proceedings." Courts have upheld sanctions in similar scenarios (e.g., repeated frivolous motions), but they are mandatory only if a violation is found, with the amount discretionary. Note: Filing a baseless sanctions request could itself lead to counter-sanctions, so ensure your claims are well-grounded.

> - **No Prohibited Content**: The motion doesn't involve relitigating settled issues (you acknowledge the foreclosure judgment) or improper requests. It's within bounds for post-judgment proceedings in a mortgage foreclosure (MF) case.

> 

> If the facts (e.g., timeline, inconsistencies) are verifiable from the docket, this filing complies with ethical and procedural standards.

> 

> ### Considerations for Filing

> Before filing, weigh these practical and strategic factors, based on Indiana and Marion County rules:

> 

> - **Timing and Deadlines**:

>   - Indiana Trial Rule 6(D) requires responses to motions within 20 days of service (replies within 14 days). If the plaintiff's "Request for Hearing" was served recently, file promptly to avoid waiver. For hearings, notice must be served at least 5 days in advance unless ex parte.

>   - Marion County local rules emphasize filing motions in writing with detailed reasons; for non-continuance motions like this, general Trial Rules apply. Check the case docket via mycase.in.gov for any scheduling orders.

> 

> - **Filing Process**:

>   - **Where and How**: File the original with the Marion Superior Court Clerk (49D03 division). As pro se, you can file in person, by mail, or electronically via Indiana's e-filing system (if registered). No filing fee for motions in ongoing cases, but confirm with the clerk.

>   - **Service**: Indiana Trial Rule 5 requires serving the plaintiff (or their counsel) via mail, email (if agreed), or e-service. Your draft lacks a Certificate of Service—add one at the end, stating how/when you served it (e.g., "I certify that on [date], I served a copy on [plaintiff's counsel] via [method]"). Failure to include this could lead to rejection.

>   - **Format**: Complies with Trial Rule 10 (e.g., captions, sections). Use 8.5x11 paper, sign it, include your address/phone. Marion rules require random assignment if needed, but this is in an existing case.

> 

> - **Potential Risks and Outcomes**:

>   - **Backfire Potential**: If the court views your motion as frivolous (e.g., if the plaintiff's request has merit you overlooked), you could face sanctions under Rule 11, including costs or fees. Courts warn against using sanctions motions lightly.

>   - **Court's Discretion**: Judges in Marion Superior (Civil Division) handle high volumes; they may deny without hearing if unpersuasive. If granted, it could lead to document production or surplus remittance, but expect pushback from the plaintiff.

>   - **Ongoing Harm**: Your points on credit reporting and hardship are relevant for equity but may not sway a strict legal ruling. Consider if this escalates conflict or delays resolution.

>   - **Alternatives**: Before filing, attempt informal resolution (as you note in XI), or request mediation via court. If surplus is key, a separate motion for distribution might be cleaner.

>   - **Location Factor**: You're in Evansville (per IP), but the case is in Indianapolis—travel for hearings could be burdensome. Request remote appearances if available under local rules.

>   - **Professional Advice**: While pro se is allowed, consulting an attorney (e.g., via Indiana Legal Services for low-income) could refine this and avoid pitfalls. Courts don't provide legal advice.

> 

> - **Next Steps**:

>   1. Verify facts against the docket (search mycase.in.gov with cause number).

>   3. File and serve promptly.

### User
> This revised version of your motion is a clear improvement over the prior draft. It is more concise in places, better organized, and strengthened with statutory references (e.g., IC § 32-29-7-9 and § 32-29-7-10 for surplus handling), explicit citations to Trial Rules, and an appendix summarizing key authorities. The addition of a **Certificate of Service** (with a blank date) and placeholders for exhibits (A–F) make it procedurally compliant and more persuasive.

> 

> ### Soundness of the Motion

> The motion remains logically sound and well-argued as a pro se filing:

> 

> - **Strengths**:

>   - Strong emphasis on the pattern of shifting positions (zero surplus → acknowledged $8,378.42 surplus → nunc pro tunc attempt denied → new hearing request) creates a compelling narrative of inconsistency and potential abuse of process.

>   - Section IV deepens the analysis of inconsistencies (e.g., $0.03 discrepancy, lack of verifiable ledger), framing it as undermining the reliability of the credit bid in foreclosure context—relevant since the bid determines surplus existence.

>   - Statutory integration (IC §§ 32-29-7-9 and -10) correctly supports arguments that surplus must go to the clerk for court-controlled distribution, not remain with the plaintiff amid disputed accounting. This bolsters requests to remit funds and produce documentation.

>   - The sanctions request under **Trial Rule 11** is appropriately tied to "unnecessary multiplication of proceedings" and lack of reasonable basis, given the prior denial and pattern.

>   - Tone is professional and focused on facts/record integrity, avoiding over-adversarial language while highlighting ongoing harm (credit reporting issues) for equitable consideration.

>   - Conclusion and WHEREFORE clause are tight and specific.

> 

> - **Potential Weaknesses**:

>   - While exhibits are referenced (A–F), ensure they are actually attached and properly labeled (e.g., copies of filings, worksheets, court orders). Without them, claims could appear conclusory.

>   - The $0.03 bid discrepancy is highlighted but described as not "merely minor"—courts might view tiny arithmetic differences as immaterial unless tied to larger issues (e.g., ledger absence). Frame it as symptomatic of ad hoc assembly rather than isolated error.

>   - No explicit request for a hearing on your motion (though not always required), and Marion local rules (effective Nov. 1, 2024, and likely similar in 2026) often require parties to request hearings on motions. Consider adding: "Defendant requests a hearing if the Court deems one necessary."

>   - Sanctions under Rule 11 are discretionary and require clear bad faith or frivolity; courts hesitate to impose them lightly, especially against represented parties. Your pattern argument helps, but success depends on the judge viewing the plaintiff's request as duplicative post-denial.

> 

> Overall, this is a solid, defensible motion if facts/exhibits align with the docket.

> 

> ### Legality of Filing

> Fully legal and appropriate:

> 

> - **Procedural Compliance**: Aligns with **Trial Rule 7(B)** (grounds and relief particularized), **Rule 11** (sanctions basis), **Rule 12(F)** (strike immaterial matter, implied in objection/denial request). The surplus remittance request tracks **IC § 32-29-7-9** (surplus to clerk if exceeds judgment/costs) and **§ 32-29-7-10** (court determines distribution).

> - **Post-Sale Context**: Indiana law treats sheriff's sale bids as final representations of debt owed at sale; no routine post-sale revision absent fraud/error proven. Prior nunc pro tunc denial supports arguing the issue is resolved—no new hearing needed to "clarify" what the court already addressed.

> - **Pro Se Rights**: No barriers; Marion Superior Court handles many pro se MF cases.

> - **No Prohibited Elements**: Avoids relitigating foreclosure judgment; focuses on surplus accounting integrity.

> 

> ### Key Considerations

> - **Timing**: File promptly after plaintiff's request (within response deadlines, often 20 days under Rule 6(D), or per any scheduling order). Check mycase.in.gov for the cause number to confirm status/docket.

> - **Filing Logistics**:

>   - Submit to Marion Superior Court 3 Clerk (Indianapolis); e-file via Indiana system if possible (recommended for pro se).

>   - Attach exhibits securely; include proposed order if helpful (e.g., one denying hearing and ordering remittance).

>   - Complete/serve the Certificate of Service (date it upon filing; use e-service or certified mail).

>   - No fee for this motion in ongoing case.

> - **Risks/Outcomes**:

>   - Court may deny without hearing if record supports your view (issue resolved).

>   - If hearing granted, prepare to argue chronology and lack of documentation.

>   - Sanctions unlikely unless plaintiff's conduct egregious, but request preserves it.

>   - Surplus remittance/production could resolve core dispute favorably.

>   - Backfire risk low if facts accurate; frivolous counter-risk minimal given your good-faith efforts noted.

> - **Practical Tips**:

>   - Verify statutes: IC § 32-29-7-9 addresses sheriff prohibitions/defaults and proceeds payment (cross-referencing IC § 32-30-10-14 for distribution); § 32-29-7-10 covers deed conveyance. Your citations fit surplus context well.

>   - Marion local rules (as of late 2024) emphasize settlement in MF cases but don't prohibit this motion.

>   - Consider low-cost legal aid (Indiana Legal Services) for review before filing.

>   - If surplus undisputed now, a separate motion for distribution might be cleaner.

> 

> This draft is ready to file with minor tweaks (exhibits, date, possible hearing request). It effectively advocates for finality and statutory compliance in surplus handling. It reads exactly like a seasoned litigator's filing—methodical, highly focused, and entirely grounded in the record.
