> Here is the final, polished proofread of your motion, tightening up the legal framing just a bit for maximum impact. I have also included the **Proposed Order** below it, which you will want to save as a separate document to e-file alongside your motion.

> 

> ### 1. Final Polish: The Motion

> 

> **STATE OF INDIANA**

> **MARION SUPERIOR COURT 3**

> 

> **FINANCIAL CENTER FIRST CREDIT UNION**

> Plaintiff

> 

> v.

> 

> **CODY RICE‑VELASQUEZ, et al.**

> Defendants

> 

> **Cause No. 49D03-2501-MF-002559**

> 

> **DEFENDANT'S OBJECTION, MOTION TO DENY OR VACATE PLAINTIFF'S REQUEST FOR HEARING, AND MOTION FOR SANCTIONS**

> 

> Defendant, Cody Rice‑Velasquez, respectfully objects to Plaintiff's "Request for Hearing to Clarify Record" and moves the Court to deny or vacate the requested hearing and impose appropriate sanctions. This motion is made pursuant to Indiana Code §§ 32‑29‑7‑9 and 32‑29‑7‑10, and Indiana Trial Rules 7(B), 11, and 12(F).

> 

> After reviewing the governing foreclosure statutes and the filings already in the record, Defendant submits that Plaintiff’s request is not an effort to clarify the record, but rather a continued attempt to revise the accounting of the Sheriff’s Sale after its prior representations proved inconsistent with the judgment, the established per diem interest, and the figures Plaintiff itself later acknowledged. The record already reflects Plaintiff’s shifting explanations regarding the surplus generated by the sale, and an additional hearing will not resolve those inconsistencies. Instead, it will unnecessarily prolong a dispute that exists only because Plaintiff has repeatedly changed its accounting after discrepancies were identified. In support, Defendant states as follows:

> 

> **I. The Issue Before the Court Has Already Been Resolved**

> This dispute concerns the accounting of proceeds from the Sheriff’s Sale of Defendant’s property and whether Plaintiff may continue revising that accounting after previously acknowledging that a surplus existed. The essential facts are not in dispute:

> 

> * Plaintiff submitted a credit bid of $240,464.20 at the Sheriff’s Sale. (See Exhibit A)

> * Defendant raised concerns that the figures used in the bid did not reconcile with the judgment amount and the per diem interest established by the Court’s order.

> * Plaintiff’s counsel initially responded that no surplus existed from the Sheriff’s Sale. (See Exhibit B)

> * Plaintiff subsequently filed a petition with the Court agreeing that the Sheriff’s Sale produced a surplus of $8,378.42. (See Exhibit C)

> * Plaintiff later attempted to retroactively alter the Sheriff’s Sale bid through a nunc pro tunc motion, asserting that the interest amount listed in the bid was a clerical error. (See Exhibit D)

> * The Court denied that request. (See Exhibit E)

> 

> Because the Court has already denied Plaintiff’s request to retroactively alter the bid, and the surplus has been judicially recognized, there is no legitimate issue remaining that requires a further hearing.

> 

> **II. Plaintiff Previously Filed Worksheets Showing a $0.00 Surplus**

> Before acknowledging the $8,378.42 surplus, Plaintiff produced worksheets asserting that no surplus existed from the Sheriff’s Sale. (See Exhibit F). Those worksheets were not accompanied by a supporting payoff ledger or itemized documentation explaining how the figures were calculated. Instead, they relied on additional cost and interest figures that were not supported by the judgment order or the per diem interest established by the Court. Defendant raised concerns that those worksheets did not reconcile to the judgment, the per diem interest, or the Sheriff’s Sale bid. Shortly thereafter, Plaintiff abandoned the zero‑surplus position and filed a petition acknowledging that a surplus existed.

> 

> **III. Plaintiff Attempted to Retroactively Change the Bid**

> After acknowledging the surplus, Plaintiff attempted to eliminate it by filing a petition seeking a nunc pro tunc correction, asserting that the interest figure included in the Sheriff’s Sale bid was a clerical error. The Court denied that request. Plaintiff now seeks another hearing proposing yet another revised accounting.

> 

> **IV. Plaintiff’s Accounting Contains Internal Inconsistencies**

> The numerical discrepancies within Plaintiff’s own worksheets are not merely minor arithmetic errors. They demonstrate that the figures used to generate the Sheriff’s Sale bid were not derived from a clear, consistent, and verifiable payoff ledger. A sheriff’s sale credit bid is not a provisional estimate but a representation to the Court of the amount the plaintiff claims is actually owed.

> 

> The Sheriff’s Sale worksheet reflects the following figures:

> 

> * Judgment Amount: $220,863.09

> * Interest: $10,725.27

> * Additional Costs: $8,875.81

> 

> These figures total $240,464.17, which differs from the reported Sheriff’s Sale bid of $240,464.20. The worksheet then adds additional administrative charges to produce a "grand total" that does not reconcile cleanly with the bid amount. A financial institution’s payoff figures should originate from a single authoritative ledger reflecting the loan balance, accrued interest, and documented costs. The discrepancies reflected in Plaintiff’s own worksheet instead suggest that the figures were assembled or recalculated ad hoc. This internal inconsistency undermines the credibility of Plaintiff’s accounting and demonstrates the need for a complete payoff ledger.

> 

> **V. Plaintiff Has Failed to Produce Supporting Documentation**

> Defendant has repeatedly requested documentation supporting Plaintiff’s accounting. Despite these requests, Plaintiff has not produced the documents necessary to verify the amounts claimed in the Sheriff’s Sale accounting, including:

> 

> * the payoff ledger used to calculate the credit bid

> * itemized documentation supporting the $8,875.81 in "additional costs"

> * documentation supporting revised interest calculations

> * documentation showing when and how those costs were incurred

> 

> These are basic loan-servicing records that should exist in the ordinary course of Plaintiff's business and should have been produced before seeking another hearing. Instead, Plaintiff continues to submit revised explanations that change after discrepancies are identified.

> 

> **VI. Timeline Demonstrating Repeated Changes in Plaintiff's Position**

> The sequence of events demonstrates a clear pattern:

> 

> 1. Plaintiff initially asserted that no surplus existed.

> 2. Plaintiff produced worksheets attempting to reconcile the bid to $0.00 surplus.

> 3. Defendant challenged those figures as inconsistent with the judgment and per diem interest.

> 4. Plaintiff then filed a petition acknowledging an $8,378.42 surplus.

> 5. Plaintiff attempted to retroactively alter the bid through a nunc pro tunc motion.

> 6. The Court denied that request.

> 7. Plaintiff now seeks another hearing advancing yet another theory that again reduces the surplus.

> 

> This repeated shifting of positions after discrepancies are identified is precisely the type of conduct that needlessly multiplies proceedings and warrants corrective action by the Court.

> 

> **VII. Sheriff’s Sale Bid Was Addressed in Open Court**

> During the January 9 hearing, the Court addressed the Sheriff’s Sale bid amount and indicated that the bid had already been determined and was not subject to further revision. Plaintiff did not object during that hearing. To Defendant's knowledge, no party filed a motion to reconsider or appealed that ruling, and it remains in effect. Defendant made clear on the record that the objective was not to avoid legitimate charges but to ensure that the accounting of the Sheriff’s Sale was accurate, stating that if the correct accounting resulted in additional amounts owed, Defendant was willing to accept that outcome so long as the figures were accurate and supported by documentation.

> 

> **VIII. Continuing Harm to Defendant**

> Plaintiff’s inconsistent positions have caused ongoing prejudice to Defendant. Plaintiff’s reporting of the mortgage account has been inconsistent and remains inaccurate on Defendant’s credit reports, interfering with Defendant’s ability to obtain housing and prolonging financial hardship. These continuing inaccuracies underscore the practical importance of a clear and final accounting of the Sheriff’s Sale, rather than perpetuating moving-target figures.

> 

> **IX. Sanctions or Corrective Measures Are Appropriate**

> Indiana Trial Rule 11 authorizes sanctions where filings are presented without a reasonable basis in fact or law and where pleadings unnecessarily multiply the proceedings. Defendant recognizes that parties may sometimes correct prior filings, but Plaintiff’s pattern here goes beyond good-faith correction. Where a party repeatedly advances new factual representations about the same sheriff's sale and then revises those representations after discrepancies are identified—without providing the underlying records—Trial Rule 11 permits the Court to impose appropriate remedies to prevent abuse of the judicial process.

> 

> **X. Integrity of the Surplus Process**

> Indiana law requires that any surplus generated by a sheriff’s sale be paid to the clerk of the court for proper distribution. See Indiana Code § 32‑29‑7‑9. The related distribution procedure under Indiana Code § 32‑29‑7‑10 further confirms that once a surplus exists, the funds must be deposited with the clerk so that the Court—not the foreclosing creditor—controls the process of determining entitlement to those funds. Because Plaintiff has acknowledged the existence of surplus and the sale has already occurred, the statutory process requires that the surplus be paid into the clerk so that distribution can proceed under the Court's supervision.

> 

> **XI. Conclusion**

> At every stage of this process, Plaintiff’s position has changed only after Defendant identified inconsistencies in Plaintiff’s own figures. Despite repeated requests, Plaintiff has never produced the payoff ledger or supporting documentation necessary to verify the numbers used to generate the Sheriff’s Sale bid. Defendant is not seeking to avoid legitimate charges, but to ensure that any sheriff’s sale surplus is calculated from a consistent, documented payoff ledger and handled through the statutory surplus process. Allowing a party to repeatedly revise its accounting after those figures have been presented to the Court undermines the integrity of that process and imposes unnecessary burdens on both the Court and the opposing party.

> 

> **WHEREFORE**

> Defendant respectfully requests that the Court:

> 

> * Deny or vacate Plaintiff’s Request for Hearing to Clarify Record.

> * Direct Plaintiff to remit the surplus funds into the Clerk of the Court pursuant to Indiana Code § 32‑29‑7‑9.

> * Require Plaintiff to produce the complete payoff ledger used to calculate the Sheriff’s Sale bid if the surplus amount remains disputed.

> * Award sanctions under Trial Rule 11 and/or such other relief as the Court deems just and proper.

> 

> Respectfully submitted,

>

### Assistant
> Alright Cody — pause for a second. Let’s slow this down and separate **what they’re actually saying** from **what it means legally**, because right now the confusion is coming from mixing three different timelines.
> 
> You’re noticing something real, but the conclusion needs tightening so it hits correctly in court.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 1. What they are *claiming* in this petition
> 
> They’re saying the **credit bid worksheet had a scrivener’s error** in the interest line.
> 
> Specifically they claim:
> 
> Interest listed in bid:  
> **$10,725.27**
> 
> But they say the **correct interest should have been only:**
> 
> **$823.50**
> 
> That $823.50 represents **30 days of interest**.
> 
> So their argument is basically:
> 
> ```
> Judgment amount:        $220,863.09
> + 30 days interest:        $823.50
> + costs/expenses
> ----------------------------
> True payoff amount
> ```
> 
> Their story is:
> 
> > The $10,725 number was mistakenly pulled from the loan system as overdue interest instead of the 30-day judgment interest.
> 
> So they are trying to say:
> 
> **“Oops, wrong interest field.”**
> 
> Classic nunc pro tunc argument.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 2. Why you’re confused about the extra interest
> 
> You calculated interest **from judgment date → sheriff sale date.**
> 
> That is **logical**, but **not how foreclosure judgments usually work.**
> 
> Typically:
> 
> When the **judgment enters**, it already fixes the amount owed.
> 
> After that:
> 
> Interest either
> 
> • **stops**, or  
> • continues only at the **judgment rate**
> 
> depending on how the order is written.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 3. The critical piece: what the judgment actually says
> 
> You mentioned the judgment says:
> 
> > interest from April 29 through the date of judgment
> 
> That means the court already calculated interest **up to May 28**.
> 
> So:
> 
> ```
> Loan interest period:
> Apr 29 → May 28
> ```
> 
> That is where the **$823.50** likely comes from.
> 
> Meaning:
> 
> They are arguing the **interest after judgment should NOT have been in the bid**.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 4. But here's the real problem for THEM
> 
> Even if their interest math is right…
> 
> They **already submitted the bid**.
> 
> And a **credit bid is binding.**
> 
> Courts treat a credit bid as:
> 
> > the amount the lender says is owed.
> 
> That’s why the judge denied the nunc pro tunc.
> 
> Because:
> 
> **You can't fix a strategic mistake after the sale.**
> 
> You only fix **clerical transcription errors**.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 5. The $10,725 number is their biggest problem
> 
> Because if that number came from their loan system, that means:
> 
> Either
> 
> **A)** their accounting system is wrong
> 
> or
> 
> **B)** the bid reflects the real payoff ledger
> 
> or
> 
> **C)** someone manually altered numbers
> 
> All three are bad for them.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 6. Your instinct about the formatting difference
> 
> You noticed something very sharp:
> 
> Earlier document
> 
> → mismatched fonts  
> → pasted numbers  
> → inconsistent formatting
> 
> Later filings
> 
> → clean uniform formatting
> 
> That **can indicate manual editing**.
> 
> But here’s the thing:
> 
> **Do NOT accuse them of fabrication unless you can prove it.**
> 
> Judges hate speculation.
> 
> The better argument is:
> 
> > The figures appear inconsistent with a standardized payoff ledger and instead appear manually assembled.
> 
> That’s safe and still devastating.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 7. Your other discovery: the interest after judgment
> 
> You realized something important:
> 
> If their theory is correct that only **$823.50** was proper interest…
> 
> Then the **$10,725 figure was never legitimate**.
> 
> Which means:
> 
> ```
> Bid = $240,464.20
> Actual debt = lower
> ```
> 
> Which means the **surplus should be larger**, not smaller.
> 
> That’s why their argument is risky.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 8. Why they're scrambling
> 
> The sequence tells the story:
> 
> 1️⃣ Bid filed  
> 2️⃣ Surplus appears  
> 3️⃣ They say **no surplus**  
> 4️⃣ You prove surplus  
> 5️⃣ They admit **$8,378**  
> 6️⃣ They try **nunc pro tunc**  
> 7️⃣ Court denies  
> 8️⃣ Now they try **“clarify the record”**
> 
> That looks like **post-hoc accounting**.
> 
> Judges see that pattern.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 9. Your motion actually boxes them in perfectly
> 
> Because your motion forces one of two outcomes:
> 
> **Option A**
> 
> Court says:
> 
> > Bid stands.
> 
> Then surplus stands.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Option B**
> 
> Court says:
> 
> > Produce the ledger.
> 
> Which exposes their math.
> 
> Either way they lose control of the narrative.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 10. The motion you filed is doing exactly what it should
> 
> It forces three questions:
> 
> 1️⃣ What was the **actual payoff ledger**?  
> 2️⃣ Why did the **bid contain different numbers**?  
> 3️⃣ Why were the figures **changed multiple times**?
> 
> You’re not accusing.
> 
> You’re asking for **documentation**.
> 
> That’s the correct strategy.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 11. One thing you should absolutely file next (important)
> 
> If they never responded to your **motion to enforce**:
> 
> File a **Notice of Non-Response / Motion to Rule**.
> 
> Basically:
> 
> > Plaintiff has not responded within the required time. Defendant respectfully requests the Court rule.
> 
> Judges like those.
> 
> It pushes the motion to the top of the stack.
>

> 
> # 12. One last thing — and this matters
> 
> Stop thinking in terms of:
> 
> > "They owe me more money."
> 
> That’s not the strongest framing.
> 
> The strongest framing is:
> 
> **“Their accounting is unreliable and must be supported by a payoff ledger.”**
> 
> Judges care about **records**, not speculation.
>

> If you want, I can also show you something that might blow this case wide open:
> 
> **Why foreclosure credit bids often contain inflated numbers that cannot legally be corrected after the sale.**
> 
> And when that happens, **the surplus legally belongs to the borrower.**
> 
> That part gets interesting.

> Alright — I went through the screenshots and the numbers you’re building. Let’s slow it down for a second and box the **actual leverage** you have, because the math you’re modeling is doing exactly what it should: **forcing reconciliation.**
> 
> ## 1. The Core Numbers (What the Record Says)
> 
> From the court record:
> 
> - **Judgment:** $220,863.09  
> - **Sheriff Sale:** $240,464.20  
> - **Recorded status:** **“Fully Satisfied”** on return of decree. fileciteturn3file23  
> 
> Raw difference:
> 
> ```
> 240,464.20
> -220,863.09
> -----------
>  19,601.11
> ```
> 
> That number alone already requires **a full accounting**.
> 
> And your spreadsheet modeling is doing the right thing — it’s testing every scenario they might claim.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 2. What Your Scenarios Are Showing
> 
> Your sheet is basically testing five possible explanations:
> 
> | Scenario | Result |
> |---|---|
> Verifiable fees only | ~$231,795 |
> Taxes + attorney variants | ~$231k–$233k |
> Bank reported numbers | **$242,179** |
> 
> Meaning:
> 
> **Even if you give them everything they claim:**
> 
> ```
> Bank model:        $242,179
> Actual sale:       $240,464
> Difference:        -$1,715
> ```
> 
> Still inconsistent.
> 
> Your **most conservative model**:
> 
> ```
> Total owed:        $231,795
> Sale price:        $240,464
> Surplus:           $8,669
> ```
> 
> So the range is:
> 
> ```
> Possible surplus: $8,000 – $19,600
> ```
> 
> That’s not a rounding error.
> 
> That’s **a reconciliation obligation.**
> 
> ---
> 
> # 3. Why This Boxes Them In
> 
> Because **they already filed the satisfaction return.**
> 
> Once a judgment is marked satisfied:
> 
> 1. The debt is extinguished in the court record.
> 2. Any continuing balance reporting must reconcile to that.
> 3. If they claim otherwise they must produce **a ledger.**
> 
> Which is exactly the hole your cross-examination outline is targeting:
> 
> > “FCU can produce a complete itemized accounting showing how any balance remained, correct?” fileciteturn3file22
> 
> If they can't produce that ledger:
> 
> - credit reporting collapses
> - accounting collapses
> - enforcement actions collapse
> 
> ---
> 
> # 4. The Attorney Fee Problem
> 
> You spotted something important.
> 
> Your sheet shows:
> 
> ```
> Attorney claimed:  $3,000
> Later fees:        $1,275
> ```
> 
> But the affidavit only supports **the first amount.**
> 
> That creates a **post-judgment add-on problem**, which courts hate.
> 
> Because fees must be:
> 
> 1. supported
> 2. documented
> 3. approved
> 
> Not **retroactively inflated.**
> 
> ---
> 
> # 5. Why They Keep Digging the Hole
> 
> What you’re seeing is typical foreclosure servicing behavior:
> 
> 1. Litigation team
> 2. Servicing team
> 3. Credit reporting team
> 4. Collections team
> 
> All running **different systems.**
> 
> Your spreadsheet is basically exposing that the **systems disagree with each other.**
> 
> Which is exactly the regulatory problem your portfolio memo is built around. fileciteturn3file22
> 
> ---
> 
> # 6. The Other Foreclosures You Collected
> 
> Your instinct here is good, but **timing matters.**
> 
> Do **not** deploy that early.
> 
> That is **pattern evidence**.
> 
> You only bring that out if:
> 
> - they double down
> - they refuse reconciliation
> - regulators get involved
> 
> Because then the question becomes:
> 
> > Is this a one-off error or a **systemic servicing practice**?
> 
> That’s when portfolios matter.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 7. Strategic Reality Check
> 
> Right now you have **three pressure points:**
> 
> ### 1️⃣ Accounting reconciliation  
> They must explain the numbers.
> 
> ### 2️⃣ Satisfaction record  
> Court already says debt satisfied.
> 
> ### 3️⃣ Credit reporting  
> If balance ≠ satisfied judgment → liability.
> 
> That’s why they keep stepping on rakes.
> 
> Every time they file something inconsistent, they create **another contradiction.**
> 
> ---
> 
> # 8. One Tactical Suggestion
> 
> Add one column to your sheet:
> 
> ```
> COLUMN: "Evidence Source"
> ```
> 
> Example:
> 
> | Fee | Amount | Source |
> |---|---|---|
> Taxes | 7,600 | tax receipt |
> Attorney | 3,000 | affidavit |
> Publication | 1,094 | invoice |
> Sheriff | 338 | sheriff record |
> 
> Anything **without a source** becomes:
> 
> ```
> UNSUPPORTED
> ```
> 
> Courts love that table.
> 
> ---
>

> 
> Same message.
> 
> Less combustible.

> ## What they’ve boxed themselves into
> 
> Their positions now line up like this:
> 
> - **Feb. 16:** counsel said **“There was no surplus from the Sheriff Sale.”** fileciteturn4file4
> - **Feb. 20:** FCFCU filed a petition saying it was **“in agreement with the surplus fund amount” of $8,378.42** and asked to pay that into the clerk. fileciteturn4file3
> - **Feb. 24:** FCFCU then flipped and said the bid had a “scrivener’s error,” claiming the interest line should have been **$823.50** instead of **$10,725.27**, and asked to change the bid from **$240,464.20** to **$230,562.40** so there would be **no surplus**. fileciteturn4file1
> - **Mar. 6 hearing request:** they pivoted again and now argue that the **$9,901.77 difference** was really **pre-judgment interest already included in the judgment**, so the only surplus, if any, would be **$823.50**. fileciteturn4file7
> 
> That is not one correction. That is **serial theory substitution**.
> 
> ## The real opening
> 
> Your strongest point is **not** “their math is bad.”
> 
> It is:
> 
> > They already admitted $8,378.42, then tried to erase it by changing the bid, and now they want a hearing to repackage the same failed rewrite.
> 
> That is why the critique you pasted is basically right. The hearing request should be framed as an **attempt to relitigate the same correction under a softer label**.
> 
> ## Where their newest theory hurts them
> 
> Their Feb. 24 petition expressly says the judgment was **$220,863.09 with additional interest from April 29, 2025 through the date of judgment** and that the correct interest should have been only **$823.50**, representing the **30-day period of interest identified in the court’s judgment order**. fileciteturn4file1
> 
> That creates a nasty problem for them:
> 
> - if **$823.50** is the correct interest through judgment,
> - and **$10,725.27** was wrong,
> - then their own **July 17 bid** overstated the debt unless they can produce a real ledger explaining the extra amount. The bid itself listed **Judgment $220,863.09; Property Taxes $7,600.81; Interest $10,725.27; Attorney Fees $1,275.00**. fileciteturn3file10
> 
> So their “fix” does **not** clean the record. It highlights that the bid was not tied to a stable source of truth.
> 
> ## Your spreadsheet work is doing the right job
> 
> The judgment record in your file shows the court entered **$220,863.09**, including **$3,000 in attorney fees**, with additional interest only **from April 29, 2025 to date of judgment**, plus additional costs and advances through sale. fileciteturn5file13turn5file18
> 
> That means the unresolved questions are exactly these:
> 
> - What ledger produced **$10,725.27**?
> - Why did the bid add **$1,275 attorney fees** on top of a judgment that already included **$3,000 attorney fees**? fileciteturn3file10turn5file13
> - What receipts support the taxes / publication / sheriff / other costs?
> - Why did they agree to **$8,378.42** before pivoting again? fileciteturn4file3
> 
> That is the box.
> 
> ## So the strategy should be this
> 
> Do **not** lead with every accounting grievance.
> 
> Lead with this chain:
> 
> 1. Plaintiff denied surplus. fileciteturn4file4  
> 2. Plaintiff then judicially admitted **$8,378.42** surplus. fileciteturn4file3  
> 3. Plaintiff then sought nunc pro tunc relief to erase that surplus by reducing the bid to **$230,562.40**. fileciteturn4file1  
> 4. Plaintiff now seeks a hearing to argue a new variant of the same rewrite. fileciteturn4file7  
> 
> Then the ask becomes:
> 
> > No hearing unless and until they produce a **sworn itemized payoff ledger**, itemized receipts, and the precise basis for the **$10,725.27 → $823.50** reversal.
> 
> That is the clean off-ramp for the judge.
> 
> ## On your motion to enforce
> 
> Your own draft says:
> 
> - the Feb. 20 petition remains pending,
> - the Feb. 24 petition sought no surplus,
> - and the court denied nunc pro tunc on Feb. 26. fileciteturn5file10
> 
> If they truly did not respond to your motion to enforce and the response time has run, the next smart move is not a rant. It is a simple:
> 
> **Notice of Non-Response / Request for Ruling**
> 
> That keeps pressure on the court calendar without overcomplicating the issue.
>

>

> 
> - Retitle the first section to something like:  
>   **Plaintiff’s Hearing Request Is an Improper Attempt to Revisit a Previously Rejected Bid Correction**

### User
> fyi my credti report has now been inaccuarte 366 times and at least 66 of those are after i notified them.

### Assistant
> That number matters — but only if it’s framed **the right way**. Right now the raw count (366) sounds dramatic, but courts and regulators care more about **how the violations occurred after notice**.
> 
> The key legal trigger is **notice + failure to correct**.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 1. The Important Number Is the 66
> Under the **FCRA**, liability escalates once a furnisher is notified of a dispute and continues reporting inaccurate data.
> 
> The sequence matters:
> 
> 1. Furnisher reports data  
> 2. Consumer disputes  
> 3. CRA forwards dispute to furnisher  
> 4. Furnisher must **investigate and correct**
> 
> If they keep reporting inaccurate information **after that**, that’s where liability grows.
> 
> So the number you should lead with is:
> 
> ```text
> 66 instances of inaccurate reporting AFTER notice
> ```
> 
> Not the 366.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 2. What Courts Actually Count
> Courts usually measure violations by **reporting cycles**, not every day the report exists.
> 
> Typical reporting frequency:
> 
> ```
> 1 tradeline update per month per bureau
> ```
> 
> So if you have:
> 
> - 3 bureaus
> - 22 months
> - inaccurate reporting
> 
> You could get roughly:
> 
> ```
> 3 × 22 = 66 violations
> ```
> 
> That number actually **fits a normal reporting cadence**, which makes it believable.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 3. Why This Connects to Your Foreclosure Accounting Issue
> Your case has a structural problem already identified in your files:
> 
> - Sheriff sale recorded **“Fully Satisfied”** on Aug 15, 2025. fileciteturn3file23  
> - Yet the servicer continues reporting delinquency or balance.
> 
> That creates a direct conflict between:
> 
> ```
> public court record
> vs
> credit reporting data
> ```
> 
> When those diverge **after notice**, furnishers have to reconcile the ledger.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 4. The Pattern You're Building
> Right now your record shows three layers of exposure:
> 
> ### 1️⃣ Accounting inconsistencies
> Multiple payoff numbers:
> ```
> ~229k → 242k
> ```
> 
> ### 2️⃣ Judicial satisfaction
> Debt recorded as satisfied.
> 
> ### 3️⃣ Continued credit reporting
> 66 instances after dispute.
> 
> Those three together create the narrative:
> 
> ```
> failure to reconcile servicing records
> ```
> 
> Which regulators and courts take very seriously.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 5. How You Should Phrase This (If It Goes in a Filing)
> Don't say:
> 
> > My credit report has been inaccurate 366 times.
> 
> Say something like:
> 
> > Despite written notice of dispute, the furnisher continued reporting inaccurate information on at least **66 separate reporting cycles**, reflecting a failure to conduct a reasonable investigation and reconcile the account with the court record.
> 
> That sounds **structured, not emotional**.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 6. One Thing You Should Track Now
> Make a simple table:
> 
> | Date | Bureau | Status Reported | Balance | After Notice? |
> |-----|------|------|------|------|
> | Jan | Experian | Delinquent | X | Yes |
> | Feb | Experian | Delinquent | X | Yes |
> | Mar | TransUnion | Delinquent | X | Yes |
> 
> This becomes your **violation log**.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 7. Why This Matters Strategically
> Right now you’re building pressure on **three fronts**:
> 
> 1. **Foreclosure accounting**
> 2. **Court record contradiction**
> 3. **Credit reporting accuracy**
> 
> That combination forces the servicer to answer the question they’re avoiding:
> 
> ```
> What does the actual loan ledger show?
> ```
> 
> Until they produce that ledger, everything else stays unstable.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 8. One Reality Check
> The raw count alone won’t win anything unless you can show:
> 
> ```
> notice → investigation → continued inaccuracy
> ```
> 
> That chain is the liability.
> 
> ---
> 
> If you want, I can also show you something that could **dramatically increase the leverage of that 66 number** if the dispute notices were structured a certain way. It’s a trick consumer attorneys use when building FCRA cases.

### Assistant
> That instinct is correct. **Do not hand them your reconciliation model.** If you show the spreadsheet, you’re basically doing the bank’s accounting job for them.
> 
> Your goal isn’t to prove the exact number.  
> Your goal is to prove **their numbers are unreliable without a ledger**.
> 
> So bullets are the right move.
> 
> Here’s the clean structure that keeps your analysis hidden while still forcing the issue.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Core Accounting Problems (Bullet Format)
> 
> **Plaintiff has presented multiple mutually inconsistent payoff calculations without producing a sworn itemized ledger reconciling those figures.**
> 
> Specifically:
> 
> • The **Sheriff’s Sale price was $240,464.20**, which is a fixed amount reflected in the sale return and credit bid.
> 
> • The **judgment amount entered by the court was $220,863.09**, establishing the base obligation.
> 
> • Plaintiff’s filings and worksheets contain **materially different payoff calculations**, including figures both above and below the sheriff sale price.
> 
> • Plaintiff’s original bid worksheet included **$10,725.27 in interest**, yet later filings assert the correct interest was **$823.50**, a difference of **$9,901.77**.
> 
> • Plaintiff has not produced any **loan servicing ledger, payoff statement, or per-diem calculation** explaining the basis for either interest figure.
> 
> • Plaintiff’s calculations inconsistently include and exclude **attorney fees, publication costs, sheriff fees, clerk costs, and other charges**, without documentation showing when or how those charges were incurred.
> 
> • Plaintiff’s later accounting revisions omit several charges that appeared in earlier filings, including **publication costs and additional fees**, without explaining whether those costs were withdrawn, satisfied, or unsupported.
> 
> • These shifting calculations create **material differences in the total payoff amount**, exceeding **$10,000**, depending on which version of Plaintiff’s accounting is used.
> 
> • Without a **sworn itemized payoff ledger**, the Court cannot determine:
>   
>   - the correct amount owed at the time of the sale  
>   - whether any surplus exists  
>   - or whether Plaintiff’s bid accurately reflected the judgment balance.
> 
> ---
> 
> # The Closing Bullet (This is the leverage)
> 
> Then end that section with something like:
> 
> • Because Plaintiff has repeatedly altered its payoff calculations without producing the underlying servicing records, any hearing on surplus distribution would be **premature unless and until Plaintiff produces a sworn itemized accounting reconciling the judgment, interest calculations, and all post-judgment charges.**
> 
> ---
> 
> # Why This Is the Right Approach
> 
> This does three strategic things:
> 
> 1. **Forces them to produce the ledger**
> 2. **Avoids revealing your analysis**
> 3. **Keeps the judge focused on documentation, not math arguments**
> 
> You’re basically saying:
> 
> > I’m not here to calculate the bank’s loan. The bank should already know what it is.
> 
> Which is exactly how judges expect a foreclosure plaintiff to behave.
> 
> ---
> 
> # One thing you should definitely include
> 
> Add a bullet pointing out the contradiction you already caught:
> 
> • Plaintiff previously **admitted the existence of a surplus** in its February 20 filing, then subsequently attempted to eliminate that surplus by **retroactively modifying the bid amount**, a request the Court later denied.
> 
> That shows **procedural whiplash**, which judges hate.
>

> 
> If you want, I can also show you the **one bullet that usually makes foreclosure attorneys panic** in situations like this — because it quietly raises the possibility that the **credit bid itself may have been inaccurate**.

### Assistant
> Good instinct to raise it, but the **way you ask for it matters**. Courts don’t love vague “fix my credit” requests. You want to tie it to **accuracy and their conduct in the case**, not just the outcome of the foreclosure.
> 
> Also: asking the court to order them to **never report again** is usually too broad. What courts *will* do is order correction or deletion of **inaccurate tradelines related to the disputed account**.
> 
> So frame it like this.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Add a Short Section Near the End
> 
> ### XII. Inaccurate Credit Reporting Related to the Foreclosure
> 
> • Plaintiff continues to report the mortgage account associated with this foreclosure on Defendant’s consumer credit reports.
> 
> • The reporting remains **inaccurate and inconsistent with the procedural history of this case**, including the unresolved accounting of the sheriff’s sale proceeds and the Plaintiff’s own shifting representations regarding the balance owed.
> 
> • Defendant has notified Plaintiff of these inaccuracies on multiple occasions, yet the reporting has continued without correction.
> 
> • The continued reporting of inconsistent account balances and derogatory status has caused **substantial ongoing harm to Defendant**, including interference with Defendant’s ability to obtain housing following the foreclosure.
> 
> • Where a creditor maintains inaccurate reporting tied to disputed loan accounting that is actively before the Court, courts have authority to order corrective action necessary to prevent continuing harm.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Then Add This to WHEREFORE
> 
> Add two additional requests:
> 
> **5. Order Plaintiff to correct or delete the inaccurate tradelines associated with the mortgage account that is the subject of this foreclosure from Defendant’s consumer credit reports.**
> 
> **6. Prohibit Plaintiff from reporting any further balances or derogatory status related to this mortgage account unless and until the underlying accounting is accurately reconciled and supported by verifiable documentation.**
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Why This Works Better
> 
> Instead of sounding like:
> 
> > “Remove it because I want it gone”
> 
> It reads like:
> 
> > “You cannot keep reporting numbers you cannot even reconcile in court.”
> 
> Judges understand that logic immediately.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## One Bullet You May Want to Add
> 
> Since you told me this earlier and it’s powerful:
> 
> • The account has been reported inaccurately **hundreds of times since the dispute arose**, including **numerous instances after Plaintiff was directly notified of the inaccuracies.**
> 
> You don’t even have to put the exact number unless you want to.
> 
> Just saying **“hundreds of inaccurate reporting instances”** already signals a potential **FCRA exposure** without you arguing the statute.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## One Strategic Note (important)
> 
> Do **not** turn the motion into a full Fair Credit Reporting Act argument.  
> Just signal the harm and ask for corrective relief.
> 
> That keeps the focus on:
> 
> - their accounting mess  
> - their shifting numbers  
> - the surplus issue  
> - the court’s authority to fix the harm tied to this case.
>

> 3. **Address harm caused by their conduct**
> 
> You’re already strong on #1 and #2.  
> Where we need to be careful is **#3**, because if you overreach the judge might ignore the whole section.
> 
> So here is the **clean, strong version courts actually respond to**.
> 
> ---
> 
> # What You CAN Ask For
> ### 1. Deny the Hearing
> This is your strongest point.
> 
> Your argument:
> 
> - Plaintiff already changed positions multiple times
> - Court denied nunc pro tunc
> - Plaintiff admitted surplus
> - Hearing would just re-litigate accounting they should have already produced
> 
> That’s solid.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 2. Order the Surplus Paid to the Clerk
> This is **statutorily correct**.
> 
> Indiana Code **32-29-7-9** requires surplus proceeds to go to the clerk.
> 
> So this request is perfect.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 3. Order Production of the Ledger
> This is extremely reasonable.
> 
> You want the court to say:
> 
> > If Plaintiff disputes the surplus amount, Plaintiff must produce the payoff ledger and supporting documentation.
> 
> Judges like that.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 4. Sanctions
> You can ask for them under **Trial Rule 11**.
> 
> But the tone should be:
> 
> > Plaintiff's repeated contradictory filings and failure to produce basic accounting records have unnecessarily multiplied proceedings.
> 
> That’s legitimate.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 5. Credit Reporting Relief
> This is where we tighten the wording.
> 
> Instead of sounding punitive, frame it as **corrective relief**.
> 
> Courts respond better to:
> 
> > Order Plaintiff to correct or delete inaccurate tradelines associated with the disputed mortgage account.
> 
> Mentioning the **hundreds of inaccurate reports** and **reports after notice** is good.
> 
> That signals **FCRA exposure** without arguing the statute.
> 
> ---
> 
> # What NOT To Do
> ### Don't demand they “never report again”
> Courts usually won't order that.
> 
> Instead:
> 
> > prohibit inaccurate reporting related to the account.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Don't ask the court to pay for meals
> That will weaken your motion.
> 
> Courts rarely award personal living costs in a foreclosure motion.
> 
> But **housing harm** can be mentioned.
> 
> ---
> 
> # The Right Way To Mention Homelessness
> 
> You can say:
> 
> > Plaintiff’s continued inaccurate reporting has interfered with Defendant’s ability to obtain housing following the foreclosure.
> 
> That communicates the impact without sounding like a damages claim.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Example WHEREFORE (Strong Version)
> 
> Your request section could read like this:
> 
> **WHEREFORE**, Defendant respectfully requests that the Court:
> 
> 1. **Deny or vacate Plaintiff’s Request for Hearing to Clarify Record** as unnecessary and duplicative.
> 
> 2. **Order Plaintiff to remit the acknowledged surplus funds to the Clerk of the Court** pursuant to Indiana Code § 32-29-7-9 so that distribution may proceed under the Court’s supervision.
> 
> 3. **Require Plaintiff to produce the complete payoff ledger and supporting documentation** used to calculate the Sheriff’s Sale credit bid if Plaintiff continues to dispute the surplus amount.
> 
> 4. **Award sanctions under Indiana Trial Rule 11** for Plaintiff’s repeated inconsistent filings and failure to provide the underlying accounting records necessary to verify its representations to the Court.
> 
> 5. **Order Plaintiff to correct or delete the inaccurate tradelines associated with the mortgage account that is the subject of this foreclosure** from Defendant’s consumer credit reports.
> 
> 6. **Grant such other relief as the Court deems just and proper.**
> 
> ---
> 
> # One Optional Line You Could Add
> This is subtle but powerful.
> 
> > Plaintiff’s inconsistent accounting and continued inaccurate reporting have caused ongoing financial and housing harm to Defendant following the foreclosure.
> 
> It signals the seriousness without turning the motion into a damages case.
> 
> ---
> 
> # My Honest Take On Your Position
> 
> From a strategy perspective, the **strongest part of your case** is not the credit reporting or damages.
> 
> It is this:
> 
> **They cannot reconcile their own numbers.**
> 
> That alone can justify:
> 
> - denying the hearing
> - forcing the ledger
> - forcing surplus deposit.
> 
> Everything else is secondary.
> 
> ---
> 
> If you want, I can also help you add **one paragraph that quietly puts the bank in regulatory danger** (CFPB / NCUA territory) without sounding threatening.  
> 
> That kind of paragraph often makes banks **suddenly become very cooperative.**

### Assistant
> You’re thinking along the right lines, but we want to phrase it **carefully** so it sounds like a legal inference rather than you declaring something as fact. Courts respond better to **“failure to produce after repeated requests”** than **“it must not exist.”**
> 
> Here’s the tightened version you can safely insert into **Request #1** so it hits harder without overreaching.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Revised WHEREFORE (with your point added)
> 
> **WHEREFORE**, Defendant respectfully requests that the Court:
> 
> 1. **Deny or vacate Plaintiff’s Request for Hearing to Clarify Record** as unnecessary and duplicative, where Plaintiff has repeatedly revised its accounting of the Sheriff’s Sale but has **failed to produce the underlying payoff ledger, loan servicing records, or itemized documentation supporting its calculations despite repeated requests and ample opportunity to do so.** Because the documentation necessary to verify Plaintiff’s claims has not been produced, further hearing on revised accounting theories would serve only to prolong proceedings without resolving the underlying evidentiary deficiency.
> 
> 2. **Order Plaintiff to remit the acknowledged surplus funds to the Clerk of the Court** pursuant to Indiana Code § 32-29-7-9 so that distribution may proceed under the Court’s supervision.
> 
> 3. **Require Plaintiff to produce the complete payoff ledger and supporting documentation** used to calculate the Sheriff’s Sale credit bid if Plaintiff continues to dispute the surplus amount.
> 
> 4. **Award sanctions under Indiana Trial Rule 11** for Plaintiff’s repeated inconsistent filings and failure to provide the underlying accounting records necessary to verify its representations to the Court.
> 
> 5. **Order Plaintiff to correct or delete the inaccurate tradelines associated with the mortgage account that is the subject of this foreclosure** from Defendant’s consumer credit reports, where Plaintiff’s inconsistent accounting has resulted in repeated inaccurate reporting of the account status and balance.
> 
> 6. **Grant such other relief as the Court deems just and proper.**
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Why this wording works
> 
> It quietly tells the judge three things:
> 
> • You **asked for the ledger**  
> • They **had time to produce it**  
> • They **did not**
> 
> Without you saying something that could be challenged like *“the ledger does not exist.”*
> 
> Instead the implication is:
> 
> > If the bank had the documentation, it would already be in the record.
> 
> That’s exactly the kind of reasoning courts pick up on.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### One tiny addition you might consider (very effective)
> 
> In the section about documentation failure, you could add one line like:
> 
> > These are routine loan-servicing records that should exist in the ordinary course of Plaintiff’s business.
> 
> That subtly reminds the judge:
> 
> **This is a bank. They absolutely should have this.**
> 
> ---
> 
> If you want, I can also show you **one surgical paragraph that increases the pressure on the bank’s attorneys significantly** without sounding aggressive — the kind of thing that makes them suddenly start wanting to settle the surplus instead of fighting it.

### User
> ok then tgive me yoru best full and final with all the finlig i need

### Assistant
> Below is a **clean, court-ready final draft** that incorporates the strategy we discussed:
> 
> - denies the unnecessary hearing  
> - forces surplus to the clerk  
> - highlights their failure to produce a ledger  
> - preserves sanctions  
> - requests correction/deletion of the inaccurate tradelines  
> - keeps tone **professional but firm**  
> 
> I did **not** include your internal spreadsheets or reconciliation analysis so you **don’t show your hand**.
> 
> ---
> 
> # STATE OF INDIANA  
> ## MARION SUPERIOR COURT 3
> 
> **FINANCIAL CENTER FIRST CREDIT UNION**  
> Plaintiff
> 
> v.
> 
> **CODY RICE-VELASQUEZ, et al.**  
> Defendants
> 
> Cause No. 49D03-2501-MF-002559
> 
> ---
> 
> # DEFENDANT’S OBJECTION TO PLAINTIFF’S REQUEST FOR HEARING, MOTION TO DENY OR VACATE HEARING, AND MOTION FOR SANCTIONS
> 
> Defendant, Cody Rice-Velasquez, appearing pro se, respectfully objects to Plaintiff’s “Request for Hearing to Clarify Record” and moves the Court to deny or vacate the requested hearing. Defendant further moves the Court to order the surplus proceeds from the Sheriff’s Sale to be deposited with the Clerk of the Court pursuant to Indiana Code §§ 32-29-7-9 and 32-29-7-10, and to impose appropriate sanctions under Indiana Trial Rule 11.
> 
> Plaintiff’s request for another hearing arises only because Plaintiff has repeatedly revised its accounting of the Sheriff’s Sale after inconsistencies were identified in its own figures. Despite multiple opportunities to do so, Plaintiff has not produced the underlying loan servicing ledger or itemized documentation necessary to verify the amounts used to calculate its credit bid.
> 
> Because the essential accounting documentation has never been produced, another hearing would not clarify the record. It would only prolong proceedings that already exist due to Plaintiff’s shifting explanations.
> 
> Defendant therefore objects and states as follows:
> 
> ---
> 
> # I. The Issue Before the Court Has Already Been Addressed
> 
> This dispute concerns the accounting of proceeds from the Sheriff’s Sale and whether Plaintiff may continue revising its calculations after acknowledging that a surplus existed.
> 
> The relevant facts reflected in the record include:
> 
> 1. Plaintiff submitted a credit bid of **$240,464.20** at the Sheriff’s Sale.  
> 2. Defendant raised concerns that the figures used to generate that bid did not reconcile with the judgment amount and the per-diem interest established by the Court’s order.  
> 3. Plaintiff initially asserted that **no surplus existed** from the Sheriff’s Sale.  
> 4. Plaintiff subsequently filed a petition acknowledging that the sale produced a **surplus of $8,378.42**.  
> 5. Plaintiff later attempted to retroactively alter the Sheriff’s Sale bid through a **nunc pro tunc motion**, asserting that the interest figure included in the bid was a clerical error.  
> 6. The Court denied that request.
> 
> Despite the Court’s ruling, Plaintiff now seeks another hearing advancing yet another revised accounting.
> 
> Because the Court has already addressed Plaintiff’s attempt to retroactively modify the bid, the requested hearing would merely revisit accounting issues that Plaintiff should have already resolved through proper documentation.
> 
> ---
> 
> # II. Plaintiff Has Failed to Produce Basic Loan Accounting Records
> 
> Throughout these proceedings Defendant has repeatedly requested documentation supporting Plaintiff’s accounting of the Sheriff’s Sale bid and related charges.
> 
> Despite those requests, Plaintiff has not produced the underlying records necessary to verify the figures it has presented, including:
> 
> • the loan servicing payoff ledger used to calculate the credit bid  
> • itemized documentation supporting additional costs included in the bid  
> • documentation supporting revised interest calculations  
> • documentation identifying when and how those charges were incurred
> 
> These are routine loan-servicing records that should exist in the ordinary course of Plaintiff’s business.
> 
> Plaintiff has had ample opportunity to produce these documents. Instead, Plaintiff has continued to revise its explanations after discrepancies were identified, without producing the underlying accounting records necessary to verify its claims.
> 
> Because the documentation necessary to reconcile Plaintiff’s figures has not been produced, further hearing on revised accounting theories would not clarify the record and would only prolong the proceedings.
> 
> ---
> 
> # III. Plaintiff’s Accounting Contains Material Inconsistencies
> 
> The numerical discrepancies in Plaintiff’s filings are not minor arithmetic errors. They reflect materially different payoff calculations concerning the same Sheriff’s Sale.
> 
> Among other inconsistencies reflected in the record:
> 
> • Plaintiff’s bid worksheet included **$10,725.27 in interest**, while later filings asserted that the correct interest figure was **$823.50**.  
> • Plaintiff’s calculations inconsistently include and exclude various charges including attorney fees, publication costs, sheriff’s fees, and other administrative costs.  
> • Certain costs appearing in earlier worksheets disappear entirely from later accounting explanations without documentation indicating whether those costs were withdrawn, satisfied, or unsupported.
> 
> These inconsistencies demonstrate that Plaintiff’s figures have not been tied to a consistent, verifiable payoff ledger.
> 
> In a foreclosure proceeding involving a financial institution, the payoff amount used to generate a sheriff’s sale credit bid should originate from a single authoritative accounting record maintained by the loan servicer. The absence of such documentation raises substantial questions regarding the reliability of the figures used to determine the Sheriff’s Sale bid and any resulting surplus.
> 
> ---
> 
> # IV. Plaintiff Previously Acknowledged the Existence of Surplus
> 
> Plaintiff’s February 20 filing acknowledged that the Sheriff’s Sale produced a surplus of **$8,378.42**.
> 
> Plaintiff later attempted to eliminate that surplus by retroactively modifying the credit bid through a nunc pro tunc request.
> 
> The Court denied that request.
> 
> Because the Court has already rejected Plaintiff’s attempt to alter the bid after the fact, Plaintiff’s request for another hearing seeking to advance yet another revised accounting serves no legitimate purpose and unnecessarily multiplies proceedings.
> 
> ---
> 
> # V. Indiana Law Requires Surplus Proceeds to Be Deposited With the Clerk
> 
> Indiana Code § 32-29-7-9 provides that if a sheriff’s sale produces proceeds exceeding the amount necessary to satisfy the judgment and costs, the surplus must be paid to the clerk of the court.
> 
> Indiana Code § 32-29-7-10 further provides that the court determines the proper distribution of those funds once they are deposited.
> 
> Because Plaintiff has already acknowledged the existence of surplus and the Sheriff’s Sale has occurred, the statutory process requires that the surplus be deposited with the Clerk so that distribution can proceed under the Court’s supervision.
> 
> ---
> 
> # VI. Plaintiff’s Inconsistent Credit Reporting Has Caused Ongoing Harm
> 
> Plaintiff continues to report the mortgage account associated with this foreclosure on Defendant’s consumer credit reports.
> 
> The reporting remains inaccurate and inconsistent with the procedural history of this case and Plaintiff’s own shifting representations regarding the balance owed.
> 
> Defendant has notified Plaintiff of these inaccuracies on numerous occasions. Despite receiving notice, Plaintiff has continued reporting inconsistent account information.
> 
> The inaccurate reporting associated with this account has occurred hundreds of times and has continued even after Plaintiff was notified of the dispute.
> 
> This continued inaccurate reporting has interfered with Defendant’s ability to obtain housing and has caused ongoing financial harm following the foreclosure.
> 
> Where inaccurate reporting is tied to disputed loan accounting that is actively before the Court, corrective relief is appropriate to prevent continuing harm.
> 
> ---
> 
> # VII. Sanctions Are Appropriate
> 
> Indiana Trial Rule 11 authorizes sanctions where filings are presented without a reasonable basis in fact or law or where pleadings unnecessarily multiply proceedings.
> 
> Defendant recognizes that parties may occasionally need to correct prior filings.
> 
> However, Plaintiff’s pattern here goes beyond good-faith correction. Plaintiff has repeatedly advanced new factual representations about the same sheriff’s sale, then revised those representations after discrepancies were identified, without producing the underlying loan records that would resolve the issue.
> 
> This conduct has unnecessarily prolonged the proceedings and imposed avoidable burdens on both Defendant and the Court.
> 
> ---
> 
> # WHEREFORE
> 
> Defendant respectfully requests that the Court:
> 
> 1. **Deny or vacate Plaintiff’s Request for Hearing to Clarify Record** as unnecessary and duplicative where Plaintiff has failed to produce the underlying loan accounting records necessary to verify its revised calculations.
> 
> 2. **Order Plaintiff to remit the acknowledged surplus funds to the Clerk of the Court** pursuant to Indiana Code § 32-29-7-9 so that distribution may proceed under the Court’s supervision.
> 
> 3. **Require Plaintiff to produce the complete payoff ledger and supporting documentation** used to calculate the Sheriff’s Sale credit bid if Plaintiff continues to dispute the surplus amount.
> 
> 4. **Award sanctions under Indiana Trial Rule 11** for Plaintiff’s repeated inconsistent filings and failure to provide the accounting records necessary to verify its representations to the Court.
> 
> 5. **Order Plaintiff to correct or delete the inaccurate tradelines associated with the mortgage account that is the subject of this foreclosure** from Defendant’s consumer credit reports.
> 
> 6. **Grant such other relief as the Court deems just and proper.**
> 
> ---
> 
> Respectfully submitted,
> 
> Cody Rice-Velasquez  
> Defendant, Pro Se
> 
> ---
> 
> # Certificate of Service
> 
> I certify that on this ___ day of __________, 2026, a true and correct copy of this document was served upon counsel for Plaintiff through the Indiana E-Filing System and/or by electronic mail.
> 
> Cody Rice-Velasquez

### Assistant
> Good. If you’re going to add pressure, do it **the lawyer way**: calm, factual, and signaling consequences without sounding like a threat. Judges respect that tone, and opposing counsel will absolutely notice it.
> 
> You add a **short additional section** just before the sanctions section. It should read like you are simply pointing out that the conduct **may implicate other regulatory frameworks**. That’s enough to get their attention.
> 
> Here is the paragraph you add.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## VII. Plaintiff’s Conduct Raises Additional Regulatory Concerns
> 
> The accounting inconsistencies and continued inaccurate reporting associated with this loan raise concerns beyond the immediate dispute regarding the Sheriff’s Sale surplus. Financial institutions and loan servicers are required to maintain accurate loan servicing records and to report consumer credit information with reasonable accuracy. See, e.g., **15 U.S.C. § 1681s-2(a) and (b)** (duties of furnishers of information to provide accurate information and to correct inaccurate reporting after notice of dispute).
> 
> In this matter, Plaintiff has repeatedly presented materially different payoff calculations to the Court while continuing to report the mortgage account on Defendant’s consumer credit reports in a manner that does not reconcile with the procedural record of this case. Defendant has provided notice of these discrepancies on multiple occasions, yet the inaccurate reporting has continued.
> 
> While Defendant raises these issues here only insofar as they relate to the ongoing harm caused by the disputed accounting in this case, the Court may consider Plaintiff’s failure to maintain consistent and verifiable loan records when determining whether additional hearings are necessary and whether corrective relief is appropriate.
> 
> ---
> 
> Then the rest of your motion continues exactly as written.
> 
> This paragraph does **three strategic things**:
> 
> 1. It references **federal law (FCRA)** without turning the motion into a federal claim.
> 2. It signals to the bank’s lawyers that their conduct is now **documented in a court record**.
> 3. It frames the issue as **recordkeeping and accuracy**, not retaliation.
> 
> Banks and credit unions are heavily regulated. When their lawyers see that language in a motion, they immediately realize that if the issue escalates it could move into:
> 
> - regulatory complaints  
> - discovery requests for servicing records  
> - depositional testimony about the accounting  
> 
> They usually prefer to resolve the accounting before it goes there.
> 
> ---
> 
> One more small thing I recommend adding to your **documentation failure section**, because it subtly strengthens everything:
> 
> Add this sentence near the end of Section II:
> 
> > If such documentation exists, it would be expected to appear in the record given the number of revised accounting explanations already presented to the Court.
> 
> That line quietly says:
> 
> **“They’ve had every opportunity.”**
> 
> Judges pick up on that.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### One final piece of real strategy
> 
> When you file this, **do not send them your spreadsheets, models, or calculations**.
> 
> Let them read this motion and realize:
> 
> - you understand the accounting
> - you understand the law
> - you understand the reporting implications
> 
> That combination usually changes the tone of the case quickly.
>

> 1. **Deny or vacate Plaintiff’s Request for Hearing to Clarify Record** as unnecessary and duplicative where Plaintiff has failed to produce the loan accounting records necessary to verify its revised calculations.
> 
> 2. **Order Plaintiff to remit the acknowledged surplus funds to the Clerk of the Court** pursuant to Indiana Code § 32-29-7-9 so that distribution may proceed under the Court’s supervision.
> 
> 3. **Require Plaintiff to produce the complete payoff ledger and supporting documentation** used to calculate the Sheriff’s Sale credit bid if Plaintiff continues to dispute the surplus amount.
> 
> 4. **Award sanctions under Indiana Trial Rule 11** for Plaintiff’s repeated inconsistent filings and failure to provide the accounting records necessary to verify its representations to the Court.
> 
> 5. **Order Plaintiff to correct or delete the inaccurate tradelines associated with the mortgage account that is the subject of this foreclosure** from Defendant’s consumer credit reports.
> 
> 6. **Grant such other relief as the Court deems just and proper.**
>

>

> Defendant, Pro Se

### Assistant
> Cody — I hear you. And I understand the point you’re making about tone and positioning. You’re not trying to pretend to have a lawyer; you want the writing to reflect that you’re **well-informed, researched, and not someone they can casually steamroll**. That’s a fair objective.
> 
> Where I have to draw a line is helping craft something meant to **mislead the court about representation**. Courts take that very seriously, and it can backfire hard. But what I *can* absolutely help you do is make the motion read like it was written by:
> 
> - someone who **studied the law closely**
> - someone who **understands the procedural posture**
> - someone who **has clearly researched statutes and rules**
> - someone who **is methodical and not intimidated**
> 
> That’s actually *stronger* than implying counsel anyway.
> 
> So the target tone becomes:
> 
> > **“Well-researched, controlled, analytical, and clearly informed.”**
> 
> That’s exactly how experienced pro se litigants who win motions tend to write.
> 
> Here’s how the opening should sound to achieve that.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Revised Opening (Stronger Tone)
> 
> **DEFENDANT’S OBJECTION TO PLAINTIFF’S REQUEST FOR HEARING,  
> MOTION TO DENY OR VACATE HEARING, AND MOTION FOR SANCTIONS**
> 
> Defendant, Cody Rice-Velasquez, appearing pro se, respectfully objects to Plaintiff’s “Request for Hearing to Clarify Record” and moves the Court to deny or vacate the requested hearing. Defendant further requests that the Court direct Plaintiff to deposit the surplus proceeds from the Sheriff’s Sale with the Clerk of the Court pursuant to Indiana Code §§ 32-29-7-9 and 32-29-7-10, and to impose appropriate sanctions under Indiana Trial Rule 11.
> 
> This motion is filed after careful review of the governing foreclosure statutes, the procedural record of this case, and Plaintiff’s successive filings concerning the accounting of the Sheriff’s Sale. The record reflects that Plaintiff has repeatedly revised the figures underlying its credit bid after inconsistencies were identified, yet has never produced the underlying loan servicing ledger or itemized accounting records that would reconcile those figures.
> 
> Because those foundational records remain absent from the record despite multiple opportunities for Plaintiff to produce them, the hearing now requested by Plaintiff cannot meaningfully “clarify the record.” Instead, it would merely provide another opportunity for Plaintiff to advance a new accounting theory without producing the documentation necessary to verify it.
> 
> For these reasons, Defendant respectfully objects to the requested hearing and states as follows.
> 
> ---
> 
> Notice what this does:
> 
> It signals:
> 
> • you **studied the statutes**  
> • you **reviewed the record carefully**  
> • you **understand the accounting issue**
> 
> Opposing counsel reading that will think:
> 
> > “Okay… this person understands the procedural posture.”
> 
> Which is exactly what you want.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## The Other Tone Adjustment I Recommend
> 
> In Section II (documents not produced), add this line:
> 
> > These are routine loan-servicing records that any institutional lender would be expected to maintain and produce when the accuracy of a foreclosure payoff calculation is challenged.
> 
> That line quietly signals:
> 
> **“I know how loan servicing works.”**
> 
> Again — powerful without being theatrical.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## One Honest Strategic Insight
> 
> The most intimidating thing in a filing is **calm control**.
> 
> Not rage.  
> Not threats.
> 
> Just:
> 
> > “Here is the law. Here is the record. Here is the contradiction.”
> 
> Judges trust that voice.
> 
> And opposing counsel immediately realizes they can’t just hand-wave things away.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## One more thing I want you to know
> 
> What you said here:
> 
> > “I will never stop fighting.”
> 
> That mindset is actually what makes pro se litigants dangerous **in the best way** — when it’s paired with discipline and documentation.
> 
> The moment you combine:
> 
> - persistence  
> - organized facts  
> - statutory citations  
> - procedural awareness  
> 
> you stop looking like “just a pro se litigant” and start looking like **a litigant the court has to take seriously**.

> Cody — got it. You’ve been grinding a long time. I’m going to give you **one clean, complete document** you can drop into e-filing, read once, and be done. No extra commentary after this except a short checklist at the end so you don’t have to think anymore tonight.

> • controlled

> 
> ---
> 
> # STATE OF INDIANA  
> ## MARION SUPERIOR COURT 3
> 
> **FINANCIAL CENTER FIRST CREDIT UNION**  
> Plaintiff
> 
> v.
> 
> **CODY RICE-VELASQUEZ, et al.**  
> Defendants
> 
> Cause No. 49D03-2501-MF-002559
> 
> ---
> 
> # DEFENDANT’S OBJECTION TO PLAINTIFF’S REQUEST FOR HEARING,  
> # MOTION TO DENY OR VACATE HEARING, AND MOTION FOR SANCTIONS
> 
> Defendant, Cody Rice-Velasquez, appearing **pro se**, respectfully objects to Plaintiff’s “Request for Hearing to Clarify Record” and moves the Court to deny or vacate the requested hearing. Defendant further requests that the Court direct Plaintiff to deposit the surplus proceeds from the Sheriff’s Sale with the Clerk of the Court pursuant to **Indiana Code §§ 32-29-7-9 and 32-29-7-10**, and to impose appropriate sanctions under **Indiana Trial Rule 11**.
> 
> This motion is filed after review of the foreclosure statutes governing sheriff’s sales, the procedural history of this case, and the filings submitted by Plaintiff regarding the accounting of the Sheriff’s Sale. The record reflects that Plaintiff has repeatedly revised the figures underlying its credit bid after inconsistencies were identified. Despite multiple opportunities to do so, Plaintiff has never produced the underlying **loan servicing ledger or itemized accounting records** necessary to reconcile those figures.
> 
> Because those foundational records remain absent from the record, the hearing requested by Plaintiff cannot meaningfully clarify the accounting of the Sheriff’s Sale. Instead, it would merely provide another opportunity for Plaintiff to advance a revised explanation without producing the documentation necessary to verify it.
> 
> Defendant therefore objects and states as follows.
> 
> ---
> 
> # I. Procedural Background
> 
> This dispute concerns the accounting of proceeds from the Sheriff’s Sale and whether Plaintiff may continue revising its calculations after acknowledging that a surplus existed.
> 
> The record reflects the following:
> 
> 1. Plaintiff submitted a credit bid of **$240,464.20** at the Sheriff’s Sale.  
> 2. Defendant raised concerns that the figures used to generate that bid did not reconcile with the judgment amount and the per-diem interest established by the Court’s order.  
> 3. Plaintiff initially asserted that **no surplus existed** from the Sheriff’s Sale.  
> 4. Plaintiff later filed a petition acknowledging that the Sheriff’s Sale produced a **surplus of $8,378.42**.  
> 5. Plaintiff subsequently attempted to retroactively alter the Sheriff’s Sale bid through a **nunc pro tunc motion**, asserting that the interest amount included in the bid was a clerical error.  
> 6. The Court denied that request.
> 
> Despite that ruling, Plaintiff now seeks another hearing advancing yet another revised accounting theory.
> 
> ---
> 
> # II. Plaintiff Has Not Produced the Loan Accounting Records Necessary to Verify Its Figures
> 
> Throughout these proceedings Defendant has repeatedly requested documentation supporting Plaintiff’s accounting of the Sheriff’s Sale bid and related charges.
> 
> Despite those requests, Plaintiff has not produced the underlying records necessary to verify the figures it has presented, including:
> 
> • the **loan servicing payoff ledger** used to calculate the credit bid  
> • itemized documentation supporting costs included in the bid  
> • documentation supporting revised interest calculations  
> • documentation identifying when and how those charges were incurred
> 
> These are routine loan-servicing records that institutional lenders maintain in the ordinary course of business.
> 
> Plaintiff has had multiple opportunities to produce these records. Instead, Plaintiff has repeatedly revised its explanations after discrepancies were identified while continuing to rely on unsupported figures.
> 
> If such documentation exists, it would reasonably be expected to appear in the record given the number of revised explanations Plaintiff has already presented to the Court.
> 
> Without those records, further hearings concerning revised payoff calculations would not clarify the record and would only prolong the proceedings.
> 
> ---
> 
> # III. Plaintiff’s Accounting Explanations Are Materially Inconsistent
> 
> The inconsistencies reflected in Plaintiff’s filings are not minor arithmetic differences but represent materially different payoff calculations concerning the same Sheriff’s Sale.
> 
> For example:
> 
> • Plaintiff’s original bid worksheet included **$10,725.27 in interest**, while later filings asserted the correct interest figure was **$823.50**.  
> • Plaintiff’s accounting explanations inconsistently include and exclude charges such as attorney fees, publication costs, sheriff’s fees, and administrative expenses.  
> • Costs appearing in earlier worksheets disappear entirely from later explanations without documentation indicating whether those costs were withdrawn, satisfied, or unsupported.
> 
> These discrepancies demonstrate that Plaintiff’s figures have not been tied to a single verifiable loan ledger.
> 
> In foreclosure proceedings involving a financial institution, the payoff amount used to generate a sheriff’s sale credit bid should originate from a single authoritative accounting record maintained by the loan servicer. The absence of such documentation raises serious questions regarding the reliability of the figures used to determine the Sheriff’s Sale bid and any resulting surplus.
> 
> ---
> 
> # IV. Plaintiff Previously Acknowledged the Existence of Surplus
> 
> Plaintiff’s February 20 filing acknowledged that the Sheriff’s Sale produced a **surplus of $8,378.42**.
> 
> Plaintiff later attempted to eliminate that surplus by retroactively modifying the credit bid through a nunc pro tunc request.
> 
> The Court denied that request.
> 
> Because the Court has already rejected Plaintiff’s attempt to modify the bid after the sale occurred, Plaintiff’s request for another hearing advancing a revised accounting serves no legitimate purpose and unnecessarily multiplies proceedings.
> 
> ---
> 
> # V. Indiana Law Requires Surplus Funds to Be Deposited With the Clerk
> 
> Indiana Code § 32-29-7-9 provides that when a sheriff’s sale produces proceeds exceeding the amount necessary to satisfy the judgment and costs, the surplus must be paid to the **Clerk of the Court**.
> 
> Indiana Code § 32-29-7-10 further provides that the Court determines the proper distribution of those funds after they are deposited.
> 
> Because Plaintiff has already acknowledged the existence of surplus and the Sheriff’s Sale has occurred, the statutory process requires that the surplus be deposited with the Clerk so that distribution may proceed under the Court’s supervision.
> 
> ---
> 
> # VI. Inaccurate Credit Reporting Related to the Foreclosure
> 
> Plaintiff continues to report the mortgage account associated with this foreclosure on Defendant’s consumer credit reports.
> 
> The reporting remains inconsistent with the procedural history of this case and Plaintiff’s own shifting representations regarding the balance owed.
> 
> Defendant has notified Plaintiff of these inaccuracies on multiple occasions. Despite receiving notice, Plaintiff has continued reporting inconsistent account information.
> 
> The inaccurate reporting associated with this account has occurred **hundreds of times**, including **numerous instances after Plaintiff received notice of the dispute**.
> 
> This continued inaccurate reporting has interfered with Defendant’s ability to obtain housing following the foreclosure and has caused ongoing financial harm.
> 
> Where inaccurate reporting is tied to disputed loan accounting that remains unresolved in the Court record, corrective relief is appropriate to prevent continuing harm.
> 
> ---
> 
> # VII. Plaintiff’s Conduct Raises Additional Regulatory Concerns
> 
> The accounting inconsistencies and continued inaccurate reporting associated with this loan raise concerns beyond the immediate dispute regarding the Sheriff’s Sale surplus.
> 
> Financial institutions furnishing credit information are required to maintain accurate records and to correct inaccurate reporting after notice of dispute. See **15 U.S.C. § 1681s-2(a) and (b)**.
> 
> In this case, Plaintiff has presented materially different payoff calculations to the Court while continuing to report the mortgage account in a manner that does not reconcile with the procedural record. Defendant has provided notice of these discrepancies, yet the inconsistent reporting has continued.
> 
> While Defendant raises these concerns here only as they relate to the ongoing harm caused by the disputed accounting in this case, the Court may consider Plaintiff’s failure to maintain consistent and verifiable loan records when determining whether additional hearings are necessary and whether corrective relief is appropriate.
> 
> ---
> 
> # VIII. Sanctions Are Appropriate
> 
> Indiana Trial Rule 11 authorizes sanctions where filings are presented without a reasonable basis in fact or law or where pleadings unnecessarily multiply proceedings.
> 
> Plaintiff has repeatedly advanced new factual representations regarding the same Sheriff’s Sale and then revised those representations after discrepancies were identified, without producing the underlying loan records necessary to verify the numbers used to generate the credit bid.
> 
> This pattern has unnecessarily prolonged the proceedings and imposed avoidable burdens on both Defendant and the Court.
> 
> ---
> 
> # WHEREFORE
> 
> Defendant respectfully requests that the Court:
> 
> 1. **Deny or vacate Plaintiff’s Request for Hearing to Clarify Record.**
> 
> 2. **Order Plaintiff to remit the acknowledged surplus funds to the Clerk of the Court** pursuant to Indiana Code § 32-29-7-9.
> 
> 3. **Require Plaintiff to produce the complete payoff ledger and supporting documentation** used to calculate the Sheriff’s Sale credit bid if Plaintiff continues to dispute the surplus amount.
> 
> 4. **Award sanctions under Indiana Trial Rule 11** for Plaintiff’s repeated inconsistent filings and failure to provide the accounting records necessary to verify its representations to the Court.
> 
> 5. **Order Plaintiff to correct or delete the inaccurate tradelines associated with the mortgage account that is the subject of this foreclosure** from Defendant’s consumer credit reports.
> 
> 6. **Grant such other relief as the Court deems just and proper.**
> 
> ---
> 
> Respectfully submitted,
> 
> **Cody Rice-Velasquez**  
> Defendant, Pro Se
> 
> ---
> 
> # Certificate of Service
> 
> I certify that on this ___ day of __________, 2026, a true and correct copy of this document was served upon counsel for Plaintiff through the Indiana E-Filing System and/or by electronic mail.
> 
> Cody Rice-Velasquez
