> # DEFENDANT’S OBJECTION TO PLAINTIFF’S REQUEST FOR HEARING AND MOTION TO ENFORCE DEPOSIT OF SURPLUS PROCEEDS
> 
> **STATE OF INDIANA  
> MARION SUPERIOR COURT 3  
> CAUSE NO. 49D03-2501-MF-002559**
> 
> **FINANCIAL CENTER FIRST CREDIT UNION**  
> Plaintiff
> 
> v.
> 
> **CODY RICE-VELASQUEZ, et al.**  
> Defendants
> 
> ---
> 
> ## DEFENDANT’S OBJECTION TO PLAINTIFF’S REQUEST FOR HEARING AND MOTION TO ENFORCE DEPOSIT OF SURPLUS PROCEEDS
> 
> Defendant, **Cody Rice-Velasquez**, appearing **pro se**, respectfully objects to Plaintiff’s “Request for Hearing to Clarify Record” and moves the Court to order Plaintiff to deposit the surplus proceeds from the Sheriff’s Sale in the amount of **$10,701.51** with the Clerk of the Court pursuant to **Indiana Code §§ 32-29-7-9 and 32-29-7-10**.
> 
> Defendant further requests that the Court deny the requested hearing because Plaintiff’s own filings already establish that surplus proceeds exist and because Plaintiff has repeatedly revised its accounting without producing the underlying loan servicing ledger necessary to verify those figures.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Reservation of Rights
> 
> Defendant’s request that Plaintiff deposit surplus proceeds with the Clerk is made solely to preserve disputed funds and prevent further multiplication of proceedings caused by Plaintiff’s shifting accounting positions.
> 
> This request is **procedural in nature** and **does not constitute a concession regarding the validity of the Sheriff’s Sale, the foreclosure judgment, or any other issue preserved for review. Defendant expressly reserves all rights and defenses.**
> 
> ---
> 
> # I. Procedural Background
> 
> This dispute concerns the accounting of proceeds from the Sheriff’s Sale and whether Plaintiff may continue revising its calculations after acknowledging that a surplus existed.
> 
> ### Chronology of Plaintiff’s Representations Regarding the Sheriff’s Sale
> 
> The record reflects the following sequence of representations made by Plaintiff regarding the Sheriff’s Sale accounting:
> 
> • **June 19, 2025** – Plaintiff submitted documentation to the Sheriff and Clerk identifying the amount of the judgment and the figures underlying its anticipated credit bid.
> 
> • **July 17, 2025** – Plaintiff submitted the Sheriff’s Sale bid in the amount of **$240,464.20**, reflecting the payoff calculation Plaintiff asserted was owed.
> 
> • **Date of Sheriff’s Sale** – Plaintiff confirmed and executed that bid at the Sheriff’s Sale using the same figures.
> 
> • **Post-sale filings** – Plaintiff filed documents with the Court reaffirming the accounting underlying the bid and asserting that the bid satisfied the debt and associated costs.
> 
> • **Subsequent filings** – Plaintiff filed additional pleadings asserting that **no surplus proceeds existed**, relying on the same underlying figures used in the credit bid.
> 
> • **January 9, 2026 hearing** – During the hearing before this Court, counsel for Plaintiff again represented that the Sheriff’s Sale produced **no excess proceeds** and that Plaintiff had bid the amount owed on the debt.
> 
> • During that same hearing, counsel further suggested that continued litigation of the accounting could potentially expose Defendant to additional liability if the figures were revisited.
> 
> • Following review of the filings, Defendant identified that the figures used in Plaintiff’s own submissions produced a surplus.
> 
> • After those discrepancies were raised, Plaintiff subsequently filed a petition acknowledging that the Sheriff’s Sale produced **surplus proceeds of approximately $8,378.42**.
> 
> • Only days later, Plaintiff filed a **nunc pro tunc motion** attempting to retroactively alter the accounting underlying the Sheriff’s Sale by claiming a clerical error affecting the interest calculation.
> 
> • The Court **denied** Plaintiff’s request to retroactively modify the accounting.
> 
> • Plaintiff now seeks a new hearing advancing yet another revised explanation of the Sheriff’s Sale figures.
> 
> This sequence reflects multiple sworn representations by Plaintiff that the Sheriff’s Sale accounting was correct, followed by a later attempt to revise those same figures after a surplus was identified.
> 
> Plaintiff’s own filings confirm that the purpose of its motion was not merely to clarify the record. In its petition and nunc pro tunc request, Plaintiff specifically asked the Court to enter an order stating that **“no surplus funds are available for distribution.”**
> 
> That request was made **after Plaintiff had already filed a petition acknowledging that surplus funds existed.**
> 
> The procedural record therefore reflects an effort to **eliminate the previously acknowledged surplus** rather than merely correct a clerical issue.
> 
> ---
> 
> # II. Statements Made During the January 9, 2026 Hearing
> 
> During the **January 9, 2026 hearing**, the Court addressed the status of the foreclosure judgment and the Sheriff’s Sale.
> 
> During that hearing the Court stated that **the judgment was complete and that no further changes would be made to the judgment amount.**
> 
> During the same hearing, counsel for Plaintiff represented to the Court that the Sheriff’s Sale produced **no excess proceeds** and that Plaintiff had bid the full amount owed on the debt.
> 
> Specifically, counsel stated that the bid covered the debt, taxes, and costs and that **“there is no excess.”**
> 
> Counsel further indicated that there was **no remaining litigation concerning the foreclosure judgment itself.**
> 
> The Court addressed the issue based on those representations at that time.
> 
> Plaintiff has since taken inconsistent positions in subsequent filings, including filings acknowledging surplus proceeds and filings attempting to revise the accounting underlying the Sheriff’s Sale.
> 
> Plaintiff’s present request therefore seeks to revisit an issue that Plaintiff previously represented to the Court as resolved.
> 
> ---
> 
> # III. Plaintiff Has Never Produced the Underlying Loan Ledger
> 
> Throughout these proceedings Defendant has repeatedly requested the documentation necessary to verify Plaintiff’s accounting of the Sheriff’s Sale.
> 
> Plaintiff has never produced the underlying servicing records necessary to verify its figures, including:
> 
> • The **loan servicing payoff ledger**
> 
> • Documentation supporting **costs included in the bid**
> 
> • Documentation supporting **interest calculations**
> 
> • Documentation identifying **when those charges were incurred**
> 
> These are routine loan-servicing records that institutional lenders maintain in the ordinary course of business.
> 
> Without those records, the Court cannot independently verify whether the figures asserted by Plaintiff originate from a consistent loan ledger or from successive recalculations after inconsistencies were identified.
> 
> ---
> 
> # IV. Plaintiff’s Own Filing Clarifies the Correct Interest Period
> 
> Plaintiff’s most recent filing clarifies that interest should be calculated **only for the thirty (30) days following the foreclosure judgment**, rather than through the date of the Sheriff’s Sale.
> 
> Defendant acknowledges that clarification because it confirms that interest should not have been carried through the Sheriff’s Sale date as earlier figures implied.
> 
> Applying Plaintiff’s own corrected methodology to the figures contained in Plaintiff’s filings results in **surplus proceeds of $10,701.51.**
> 
> Thus, even using Plaintiff’s own corrected interest period and figures, the Sheriff’s Sale necessarily produced surplus funds.
> 
> ---
> 
> # V. Indiana Law Requires the Surplus to Be Deposited With the Clerk
> 
> Indiana Code § 32-29-7-9 provides that when a sheriff’s sale produces proceeds exceeding the amount necessary to satisfy the judgment and costs, the surplus **must be paid to the Clerk of the Court.**
> 
> Indiana Code § 32-29-7-10 provides that the Court determines the proper distribution of those funds once they are deposited.
> 
> Because the Court has already denied Plaintiff’s attempt to retroactively revise the Sheriff’s Sale accounting, the bid amount remains fixed.
> 
> Using Plaintiff’s own corrected figures, the Sheriff’s Sale produced **surplus proceeds of $10,701.51.**
> 
> Under Indiana law those funds must therefore be **deposited with the Clerk so that distribution may proceed under the Court’s supervision.**
> 
> ---
> 
> # VI. Plaintiff’s Inconsistent Accounting Has Produced Ongoing Credit Reporting Harm
> 
> Plaintiff continues to furnish information concerning this mortgage account to consumer reporting agencies despite the fact that the accounting underlying the loan balance and Sheriff’s Sale bid has repeatedly changed throughout these proceedings.
> 
> The filings submitted by Plaintiff reflect materially different payoff calculations concerning the same foreclosure judgment and Sheriff’s Sale. Several submissions utilize consistent formatting and itemization of charges, while a later filing that eliminates the previously identified surplus appears in a noticeably different format and structure.
> 
> The change in formatting coincides with Plaintiff’s attempt to alter the accounting figures in a manner that eliminates the surplus previously acknowledged in its own filings.
> 
> Because the underlying loan ledger has never been produced, Defendant and the Court cannot determine whether these figures originate from a consistent servicing record or whether they were recalculated after inconsistencies were identified.
> 
> This lack of a verified accounting record has had direct consequences outside of the Court record.
> 
> The mortgage tradelines associated with this loan have continued to report to consumer reporting agencies with balances, payment histories, and account statuses that have repeatedly changed and failed to reconcile with the figures presented in Plaintiff’s own filings.
> 
> Defendant’s credit report reflects **hundreds of inconsistent reporting entries across multiple reporting periods**, including shifting balances, late payment designations, and account status changes that cannot be reconciled with any single verified loan ledger.
> 
> Defendant is prepared to submit representative excerpts of these reporting discrepancies if the Court requests them.
> 
> Because Plaintiff cannot produce a consistent loan ledger capable of verifying the balance it asserts to this Court, it cannot reasonably represent that the information it continues to furnish to consumer reporting agencies is accurate.
> 
> Financial institutions furnishing credit information must maintain accurate records and correct inaccurate reporting after notice of dispute.  
> See **15 U.S.C. § 1681s-2(a) and (b).**
> 
> Under these circumstances, continued reporting of the mortgage tradelines associated with this foreclosure while the underlying accounting remains inconsistent creates ongoing and unnecessary harm.
> 
> Accordingly, it is fair and reasonable for the Court to order that the mortgage tradelines associated with this loan be **temporarily deleted from Defendant’s consumer credit reports** until Plaintiff can produce a consistent and verifiable accounting of the balance it claims was owed.
> 
> ---
> 
> # WHEREFORE
> 
> Defendant respectfully requests that the Court:
> 
> 1. **Deny or vacate** Plaintiff’s Request for Hearing to Clarify Record.
> 
> 2. **Order Plaintiff to deposit** the surplus proceeds of **$10,701.51** with the Clerk of the Court within fourteen (14) days pursuant to Indiana Code § 32-29-7-9.
> 
> 3. **Order Plaintiff to delete** the mortgage tradelines associated with this foreclosure from Defendant’s consumer credit reports and cease further reporting related to the account unless and until Plaintiff can support such reporting with a consistent verified accounting.
> 
> 4. Grant all other relief the Court deems just and proper.
> 
> ---
> 
> Respectfully submitted,
> 
> /s/ Cody Rice-Velasquez  
> Cody Rice-Velasquez  
> Defendant, Pro Se
> 
> ---
> 
> # Certificate of Service
> 
> I certify that on this **9th day of March, 2026**, a true and correct copy of the foregoing document was served upon counsel for Plaintiff through the **Indiana E-Filing System**.
> 
>   
> /s/ Cody Rice-Velasquez
>

> 
> **STATE OF INDIANA

>

> Defendants
> 
> ---
> 
> ## DEFENDANT’S MOTION FOR SANCTIONS PURSUANT TO INDIANA TRIAL RULE 11
> 
> Defendant moves for sanctions pursuant to **Indiana Trial Rule 11** based on Plaintiff’s repeated filing of contradictory sworn statements concerning the accounting of the Sheriff’s Sale.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### I. Chronology of Contradictory Filings
> 
> The procedural record reflects the following sequence:
> 
> • Plaintiff initially filed worksheets asserting **no surplus existed**.
> 
> • Plaintiff subsequently filed a sworn petition acknowledging that the Sheriff’s Sale produced **approximately $8,378 in surplus proceeds**.
> 
> • Plaintiff then filed a **nunc pro tunc motion** attempting to retroactively alter the Sheriff’s Sale accounting by claiming a clerical interest error exceeding **$10,000**.
> 
> • The Court **denied** Plaintiff’s request.
> 
> Each of these filings was submitted under oath and relied upon by the parties and the Court.
> 
> Plaintiff has nevertheless continued presenting revised accounting theories without producing the underlying loan servicing ledger necessary to verify the figures presented to the Court.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### II. Plaintiff’s Conduct Has Multiplied Proceedings
> 
> These shifting positions have required Defendant and the Court to repeatedly address new accounting explanations unsupported by verified documentation.
> 
> Such conduct unnecessarily multiplies proceedings and requires opposing parties to continually respond to changing sworn statements.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### III. Sanctions Are Appropriate
> 
> Indiana Trial Rule 11 authorizes sanctions where filings are presented without a reasonable basis in fact or law or where pleadings unnecessarily multiply proceedings.
> 
> Because Plaintiff has repeatedly presented contradictory accounting positions without producing the underlying loan ledger that would verify its figures, sanctions are warranted.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### WHEREFORE
> 
> Defendant respectfully requests that the Court:
> 
> 1. Impose appropriate sanctions pursuant to **Indiana Trial Rule 11**.
> 
> 2. Award Defendant reasonable costs associated with responding to Plaintiff’s shifting accounting filings.
> 
> 3. Grant all other relief the Court deems just and proper.
> 
> ---
> 
> Respectfully submitted,
> 
> /s/ Cody Rice-Velasquez  
> Defendant, Pro Se

> # 2. Costs / Time You Spent Responding
> 
> For Rule 11 sanctions, courts usually award **reasonable costs**, not emotional damages.
> 
> But you absolutely can ask for **time spent responding to their filings**.
> 
> The key is to give the judge a **simple calculation**.
> 
> ### Insert this section in the sanctions motion
> 
> Add after Section II:
> 
> ---
> 
> ## IV. Reasonable Costs Incurred Responding to Plaintiff’s Filings
> 
> Plaintiff’s shifting accounting positions have required Defendant to repeatedly review filings, analyze the accounting presented, prepare responses, and appear at hearings in order to address contradictory figures presented by Plaintiff.
> 
> Defendant estimates that these efforts have required **approximately 40 hours** of work reviewing the record, analyzing the accounting presented, preparing filings, and responding to Plaintiff’s motions.
> 
> Using a conservative rate of **$75 per hour** for time reasonably expended performing legal analysis and preparing filings, Defendant’s reasonable costs incurred responding to Plaintiff’s inconsistent filings total approximately:
> 
> **$3,000.00**
> 
> This figure reflects only the time required to respond to Plaintiff’s changing accounting positions and does not include additional time required to address the ongoing credit reporting harm described above.
> 
> Defendant respectfully requests that the Court award reasonable costs in the amount of **$3,000.00** pursuant to Indiana Trial Rule 11.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Why $75/hr works
> 
> Judges hate inflated numbers.
> 
> Typical pro-se awards range:
> 
> - $50/hr
> - $75/hr
> - $100/hr
> 
> $75/hr × ~40 hrs = **$3,000**
> 
> That reads **reasonable**.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 3. Where this appears in the motion

> II. Plaintiff’s Conduct Has Multiplied Proceedings
> III. Sanctions Are Appropriate
> IV. Reasonable Costs Incurred Responding to Plaintiff’s Filings

> ```
>

> 
> ```

> 
> 1. Impose sanctions pursuant to Indiana Trial Rule 11;

> 3. Grant all other relief the Court deems just and proper.
> ```

### User
> what about all the other stuff i did origianlly i guess that is not really counting it all the stuff after the fact with these games right? if so then we are aligned.

### Assistant
> You’re thinking about it the right way. 👍 The key distinction for **Rule 11 sanctions** is **what work was caused by the opposing party’s problematic filings**, not everything you’ve ever done in the case.
> 
> ### What usually counts for Rule 11
> Courts generally award fees for **time spent responding to the specific conduct being sanctioned**—in your case, things like:
> - Reviewing their shifting surplus calculations
> - Analyzing the sheriff’s sale numbers
> - Reviewing the Jan 9 transcript to check their statements
> - Drafting the objection and motion to enforce surplus
> - Drafting the Rule 11 motion
> - Preparing exhibits tied to those issues
> - Investigating the credit-report inconsistencies linked to their numbers
> 
> ### What usually **doesn’t** count for Rule 11
> Things that happened **before the sanctionable conduct** or unrelated work, such as:
> - General case preparation earlier in the foreclosure
> - Personal research not tied to their specific filings
> - Work on unrelated legal theories or other disputes
> - Emotional time, stress, or lost opportunities
> 
> Those can matter in other contexts (appeals, separate claims, damages in another case), but **Rule 11 is narrower**.
> 
> ### Why your current approach works
> By estimating time for **the accounting dispute and the filings that followed**, you’re aligning your request with what judges expect:
> - It shows you’re **not inflating the number**
> - It ties your hours to **their conduct**
> - It gives the judge a **clear basis** to award something (even if reduced)
> 
> So if your **76-hour estimate reflects the work after the surplus/accounting issues started and their filings kept changing**, then yes—you and the calculation are aligned with how courts usually look at it.
> 
> ### Practical tip
> If the judge asks later, be ready with a **simple breakdown list** (like the table you already outlined). You don’t need a full time sheet—just categories and approximate hours.
> 
> ---
> 
> If you want, I can also help you quickly draft a **1-page “time summary” exhibit** you could attach to the sanctions motion (or bring to a hearing) so the judge sees exactly how you reached the $5,244 figure.

> 
> You **do NOT need to rewrite everything**. A few structural tweaks will dramatically strengthen it.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 1. Move “Reservation of Rights” Off Page 1
> They are **100% correct** on this.
> 
> Right now the flow is:
> 
> 1. Opening paragraph  
> 2. Reservation of Rights  
> 3. Background
> 
> That **interrupts the narrative**.
> 
> ### Fix
> Move the reservation language to either:
> 
> **Option A (cleanest)**  
> Right before **WHEREFORE**
> 
> **Option B (lawyer style)**  
> Make it a **footnote on page 1**
> 
> Example:
> 
> ```text
> 1 Defendant’s request that surplus funds be deposited with the Clerk is made solely to preserve disputed funds and prevent unjust enrichment. This request does not waive any challenge to the foreclosure judgment or related proceedings, and Defendant expressly reserves all rights and defenses.
> ```
> 
> That keeps the legal protection **without derailing the story**.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 2. Rename Section I
> Right now:
> 
> ```text
> I. Procedural Background
> ```
> 
> That’s neutral and boring.
> 
> Change it to something like:
> 
> ```text
> I. Plaintiff’s Shifting Judicial Admissions Concerning the Sheriff’s Sale Accounting
> ```
> 
> or
> 
> ```text
> I. Plaintiff’s Contradictory Judicial Admissions Concerning the Sheriff’s Sale
> ```
> 
> This **tells the judge how to interpret the timeline** before reading it.
> 
> That’s exactly how lawyers frame arguments.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 3. Strengthen the Credit Bid Language
> Your current wording:
> 
> > Plaintiff submitted the Sheriff’s Sale bid…
> 
> Make it stronger:
> 
> ```text
> On July 17, 2025, Plaintiff executed a binding credit bid in the amount of $240,464.20, thereby establishing the baseline accounting for the Sheriff’s Sale.
> ```
> 
> Why this matters:
> 
> A **credit bid is legally binding** once placed.
> 
> They can’t casually recalc it later.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 4. Explicitly Invoke Judicial Estoppel
> This is **the doctrine that stops them from changing stories**.
> 
> Add a sentence like:
> 
> ```text
> Plaintiff’s subsequent filings acknowledging surplus proceeds constitute judicial admissions. Under the doctrine of judicial estoppel, Plaintiff cannot now take an inconsistent position in order to eliminate the surplus previously acknowledged in its own filings.
> ```
> 
> Judges recognize that language immediately.
> 
> ---
> 
> # 5. Make the Nunc Pro Tunc Denial Stronger
> Right now it's just described.
> 
> Add this concept:
> 
> ```text
> The Court denied Plaintiff’s request to retroactively alter the accounting through a nunc pro tunc motion. That denial rendered the Sheriff’s Sale accounting final for purposes of determining whether surplus proceeds exist.
> ```
> 
> This reinforces:
> 
> **The court already closed the door.**
> 
> ---
> 
> # 6. Create the Missing Ledger Section
> This suggestion is **excellent**.
> 
> Add a new section:
> 
> ```text
> II. A Hearing Cannot Substitute for Missing Accounting Evidence
> ```
> 
> Then write something like:
> 
> ```text
> Plaintiff has never produced the fundamental accounting record required to verify the figures presented to this Court: the loan servicing payoff ledger.
> 
> A loan ledger is a routine business record maintained by financial institutions and reflecting all debits, credits, fees, and interest calculations applied to the loan.
> 
> Despite multiple filings revising the accounting underlying the Sheriff’s Sale, Plaintiff has never produced this basic record.
> 
> Without the underlying ledger, the Court has no reliable accounting evidence upon which a recalculation could be based. > # The “Credit Bid Finality” Paragraph (Use This)
> 
> Add this **right after the line describing the July 17 credit bid**.
> 
> ```text
> A credit bid at a judicial sale is not a tentative estimate but a binding representation of the amount the lender asserts is owed on the judgment at the time of sale. Once executed and relied upon in the confirmation of the Sheriff’s Sale, the credit bid establishes the operative accounting baseline for the transaction. Having executed that bid and subsequently represented to this Court that the bid fully satisfied the debt and produced no surplus, Plaintiff cannot now attempt to retroactively recalculate the figures in order to eliminate a surplus that its own filings later acknowledged. Permitting such a revision would undermine the finality of the Sheriff’s Sale and the integrity of the Court’s confirmation process.
> ```
> 
> Why this works:
> 
> It quietly asserts three things judges care about:
> 
> 1. **credit bids are binding**
> 2. **sales must be final**
> 3. **banks cannot rewrite the math after the sale**
> 
> You never accuse them of fraud or misconduct — but the implication is obvious.

### User
> yes, we need to make sure that we also make srue the door is closed on this hearing

### Assistant
> Alright Cody, let’s tighten the **“door-closing” part** of your motion. The goal is simple: make it **easy for the judge to deny the hearing without feeling like they’re ignoring due process**. Judges deny hearings when they can say one of these three things:
> 
> 1. **The issue is already decided.**  
> 2. **The moving party lacks evidence to support the request.**  
> 3. **The request contradicts the court’s prior orders.**
> 
> Your filing already touches all three. We just need a **short section that explicitly ties them together** so the judge has a clean justification to deny the hearing.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Add a Section Right Before WHEREFORE
> 
> Title it something like:
> 
> ### **VII. Plaintiff Has Not Established Grounds for a New Hearing**
> 
> Then insert something like this:
> 
> ```text
> Plaintiff requests a hearing to “clarify the record,” yet the procedural record already establishes the relevant facts.
> 
> First, Plaintiff executed a credit bid at the Sheriff’s Sale and repeatedly represented to the Court that the bid satisfied the debt and produced no surplus. Those representations were made in sworn filings and during the January 9, 2026 hearing.
> 
> Second, Plaintiff later filed a petition acknowledging that surplus proceeds existed. That filing constitutes a judicial admission that the Sheriff’s Sale produced funds exceeding the judgment amount.
> 
> Third, Plaintiff attempted to retroactively alter the accounting through a nunc pro tunc motion, which this Court denied. That denial left the original Sheriff’s Sale accounting intact.
> 
> Finally, despite repeatedly revising its calculations, Plaintiff has never produced the underlying loan servicing ledger that would be necessary to support any alternative accounting.
> 
> Under these circumstances, a hearing would not clarify the record. The record is already established by Plaintiff’s own filings and by the Court’s prior rulings. Granting a hearing would instead allow Plaintiff to continue revising figures that have already shifted multiple times without supporting documentation.
> 
> Because Plaintiff has not presented any new evidence capable of altering the existing record, its request for a hearing should be denied.
> ```
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Why This Closes the Door
> 
> This paragraph gives the judge a **clean reasoning path**:
> 
> - The bank **already made judicial admissions**
> - The bank **already tried to change the math**
> - The court **already denied that attempt**
> - The bank **still has no ledger**
> 
> So the judge can simply write:
> 
> > “The Court finds no basis for an evidentiary hearing.”
> 
> That’s the outcome you want.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## One Extra Sentence You Can Add (Very Effective)
> 
> Right after the paragraph about the denied nunc pro tunc motion, you could add:
> 
> ```text
> Allowing a new hearing to recalculate those figures would effectively grant the relief previously denied through the nunc pro tunc motion.
> ```
> 
> Judges **really don’t like being asked to undo their own orders indirectly**.
> 
> ---
>

>

### User
> yes

### Assistant
> Alright Cody — this is the **exact trap question dynamic** judges use in foreclosure hearings when the accounting keeps shifting. Knowing it ahead of time lets you **prepare the answer that shuts the whole thing down quickly**.
> 
> ---
> 
> # The Question Judges Ask the Bank
> 
> When lenders keep changing numbers, judges often ask something like:
> 
> > **“Where is the loan ledger that supports your calculation?”**
> 
> or
> 
> > **“Do you have the servicing ledger showing how you arrived at this balance?”**
> 
> Sometimes it’s phrased as:
> 
> > **“What document supports the balance you're asserting today?”**
> 
> This is the **moment everything pivots**.
> 
> Because one of two things happens.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Outcome 1 (Good for You)
> 
> Bank says something vague like:
> 
> > “We calculated it from the payoff.”
> 
> or
> 
> > “We don't have the ledger here today.”
> 
> or
> 
> > “We can provide it later.”
> 
> At that moment the judge realizes:
> 
> **The numbers in the filings are not tied to a concrete business record.**
> 
> That destroys credibility.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Outcome 2 (Also Good for You)
> 
> They produce a ledger **that doesn't match their filings**.
> 
> Then the judge sees:
> 
> - numbers changed
> - bid inconsistent
> - surplus misrepresented
> 
> Again, credibility problem.
> 
> ---
> 
> # The Response You Should Be Ready With
> 
> If the judge turns to you and asks something like:
> 
> > “What is your position on the accounting?”
> 
> You answer calmly and briefly:
> 
> ```text
> Your Honor, my concern is that the Plaintiff has revised the accounting multiple times but has never produced the underlying loan ledger that would verify any of those figures.
> 
> Without that ledger, the Court is being asked to rely on shifting calculations rather than the contemporaneous business record that should support the credit bid placed at the Sheriff’s Sale.
> ```
> 
> Stop there.
> 
> Do **not overtalk**.
> 
> Let the judge absorb that.
> 
> ---
> 
> # If the Judge Asks Why the Hearing Should Be Denied
> 
> Say:
> 
> ```text
> Because the Court already denied the nunc pro tunc motion that attempted to change the accounting, and without the underlying ledger there is no evidentiary basis for recalculating the Sheriff’s Sale figures.
> ```
> 
> Again — stop talking.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Why This Works
> 
> You are not accusing anyone of fraud.
> 
> You are simply pointing out:
> 
> - **numbers changed**
> - **ledger missing**
> - **court already denied revision**
> 
> Judges instinctively react to that with caution.
> 
> ---
> 
> # The Psychological Advantage You Have
> 
> The bank’s attorney wants the hearing because they think:
> 
> > “We’ll fix the math live.”
> 
> But if the judge realizes **the ledger is missing**, the hearing suddenly looks like:
> 
> > **an exercise in speculation**
> 
> Courts hate speculative hearings.
> 
> ---
> 
> # Your Core Line (Remember This)
> 
> If nothing else, remember this sentence:
> 
> > **“Plaintiff has revised its accounting multiple times but has never produced the underlying loan ledger supporting those figures.”**
> 
> That line alone undermines everything.
> 
> ---
> 
> # One More Thing After Seeing Your Filing
> 
> Your motion already shows the sequence:
> 
> 1. no surplus  
> 2. no surplus in court  
> 3. surplus admitted  
> 4. nunc pro tunc filed  
> 5. nunc pro tunc denied  
> 6. hearing requested  
> 
> That pattern looks **very suspicious to judges**.
> 
> You don’t have to say it is.
> 
> They’ll see it.
> 
> ---
> 
> # One Final Strategic Tip
> 
> If a hearing happens anyway (sometimes judges allow them out of caution), your **goal is not to argue the math**.
> 
> Your goal is to force one question:
> 
> > **“Where is the loan ledger?”**
> 
> Everything else flows from that.
> 
> ---
>

> 
> Here’s how I would use them so the exhibit hits clearly.
> 
> ---
> 
> # What These Screenshots Prove
> 
> You actually have **two separate anomalies**.
> 
> ### 1️⃣ Primary Mortgage (~$201k)
> 
> The chart shows:
> 
> - Balance around **$193k in Apr 2025**
> - Then a **sudden drop to $0**
> - Then the balance returns and continues climbing
> - Ending at **$201,220 Feb 1**
> 
> That visually suggests one of three things:
> 
> - account reported **paid/closed**
> - account **charged off then reinstated**
> - **reporting correction or data error**
> 
> Any of those contradict a **stable foreclosure accounting**.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 2️⃣ Second Mortgage (~$24k)
> 
> Same pattern:
> 
> - Balance around **$23k**
> - sudden drop to **$0**
> - then balance returns and increases again
> - now **$24,835**
> 
> Two separate loans showing the **same pattern** strengthens the argument.

> 
> > Screenshots from WalletHub credit reporting interface reflecting Financial Center First Credit Union mortgage accounts temporarily reporting a zero balance before returning to active balances.

>

> ```
> 
> You don’t need a long explanation.

> 
> ---
> 
> # Why This Is Powerful Evidence
> 
> Because it shows **data instability**.
> 
> Mortgage balances should move like this:
> 
> ```
> 190k
> 191k
> 192k
> 193k
> 194k
> ```
> 
> Not like this:
> 
> ```
> 190k
> 191k
> 192k
> 0
> 195k
> 196k
> ```
> 
> That is **reporting discontinuity**.
> 
> Judges understand that instantly.
>

>

> 
> Just say:
> 
> > "balance reporting inconsistency"
>

> ### Your logic becomes:
> 
> - They tried to insert **$1,750**
> - You’re **accepting it for purposes of the request**
> - Therefore the court can **order the deposit immediately**
> 
> It makes you look **reasonable**, and it puts pressure on them because if they object they have to explain **their own number**.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### How to phrase it (simple and sharp)
> 
> Add something like this in the motion:
> 
> > For purposes of resolving the surplus issue efficiently, Defendant does not dispute the $1,750 amount referenced by Plaintiff and requests the Court order Plaintiff to deposit that amount with the Clerk so the matter may be concluded.
> 
> Short. Calm. Deadly effective.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Why this works psychologically
> 
> Judges read it as:
> 
> - **You = cooperative**
> - **Bank = dragging feet**
> 
> Which increases the chance the judge just says:
> 
> > “Fine. Deposit the money.”
> 
> ---
> 
> ### One thing to double-check before filing
> 
> Make sure your motion request says something like:
> 
> > *“…order Plaintiff to deposit the surplus funds of $1,750 with the Clerk of the Court within X days.”*
> 
> Usually **10–14 days** is good.
>

> ok final check DEFENDANT’S MOTION FOR SANCTIONS PURSUANT TO INDIANA TRIAL RULE 11

> STATE OF INDIANA

> MARION SUPERIOR COURT 3

> CAUSE NO. 49D03-2501-MF-002559

> FINANCIAL CENTER FIRST CREDIT UNION

>  Plaintiff

> v.

> CODY RICE-VELASQUEZ, et al.

>  Defendants

> 

> Defendant, Cody Rice-Velasquez, appearing pro se, respectfully moves the Court for sanctions pursuant to Indiana Trial Rule 11 based on Plaintiff’s repeated filing of materially inconsistent sworn accounting statements concerning the Sheriff’s Sale proceeds, which have unnecessarily multiplied proceedings and required repeated responses from the Defendant.

> 

> I. Chronology of Contradictory Filings

> The procedural record reflects a series of filings by Plaintiff presenting inconsistent accounting positions regarding the Sheriff’s Sale proceeds.

> Specifically:

> Plaintiff initially filed Sheriff’s Sale worksheets asserting that no surplus proceeds existed.

> Plaintiff later filed a Petition to Pay Surplus Funds to the Clerk, acknowledging that the Sheriff’s Sale produced approximately $8,378 in surplus proceeds.

> Plaintiff subsequently filed a Motion for Nunc Pro Tunc Order, asserting that the Sheriff’s Sale accounting contained a clerical interest error exceeding $10,000, and requesting retroactive modification of the accounting.

> The Court denied Plaintiff’s request to retroactively alter the accounting.

> These filings present materially different sworn explanations of the same Sheriff’s Sale proceeds.

> 

> II. Plaintiff’s Conduct Has Multiplied Proceedings

> As a result of Plaintiff’s shifting accounting positions, Defendant has been required to repeatedly respond to new explanations concerning the same Sheriff’s Sale proceeds.

> This pattern has required additional filings and review by the Court that would not have been necessary had Plaintiff presented a consistent and verified accounting in the first instance, thereby unnecessarily increasing the burden on both the Court and the Defendant.

> Plaintiff’s repeated submission of inconsistent accounting statements concerning the same Sheriff’s Sale proceeds has required both the Court and the Defendant to repeatedly revisit issues that should have been resolved through accurate and verified accounting in the first instance. Trial Rule 11 exists in part to protect the integrity of the Court’s docket by discouraging filings that require the Court and opposing parties to repeatedly address shifting factual representations. Defendant therefore respectfully brings this motion not only to recover the reasonable costs incurred responding to these filings, but also to assist the Court in ensuring that future filings concerning the Sheriff’s Sale proceeds are supported by consistent and verified accounting.

> 

> III. Violation of Indiana Trial Rule 11

> Indiana Trial Rule 11 provides that each filing with the Court constitutes a certification that the pleading or motion is well-grounded in fact after reasonable inquiry and is not presented for the purpose of causing unnecessary delay or needlessly increasing the cost of litigation.

> Plaintiff’s repeated presentation of materially inconsistent sworn accounting positions concerning the same Sheriff’s Sale proceeds demonstrates that the representations presented to the Court cannot all  simultaneously be accurate and were not supported by a reasonable factual inquiry at the time they were filed.

> 

> IV. Costs Incurred by Defendant

> Defendant has been required to spend substantial time reviewing Plaintiff’s filings, analyzing conflicting accounting statements, preparing responses, assembling exhibits, and addressing multiple revised positions concerning the Sheriff’s Sale proceeds.

> Defendant has previously notified the Court of a documented disability, which requires additional time to carefully review financial records and verify accounting discrepancies.

> In addition, Defendant’s displacement following the foreclosure proceedings has significantly disrupted Defendant’s professional accounting and consulting work that normally billed at rates of $69 hourly or higher. As a result, Defendant’s primary current income source has been rideshare driving, which averages approximately $32 per hour.

> Defendant reasonably estimates that responding to Plaintiff’s contradictory filings and related proceedings has required approximately 55 hours of work.

> Using Defendant’s current average hourly earnings of $32 per hour, the time required to respond to Plaintiff’s shifting accounting positions represents approximately $1,760.00 in reasonable costs:

> Estimated Time: 55 hours  

> Reasonable Hourly Rate: $32/hour  

> Estimated Costs: $1,760.00~

> Defendant does not seek punitive sanctions, but only reasonable compensation for the time required to respond to filings that presented materially inconsistent sworn accounting statements and unnecessarily expanded the proceedings before the Court.

> Defendant respectfully requests that the Court consider these costs when determining appropriate sanctions pursuant to Indiana Trial Rule 11.

> V. Sanctions Requested

> WHEREFORE, Defendant respectfully requests that the Court:

> Determine that Plaintiff’s contradictory sworn accounting filings violate Indiana Trial Rule 11;

> Impose appropriate sanctions sufficient to deter further inconsistent filings;

> Award Defendant reasonable costs in an amount the Court deems appropriate.

> Alternatively, enter any other sanction or corrective order the Court finds appropriate under Trial Rule 11; and

> Grant all other relief the Court deems just and proper.

> 

> Respectfully submitted,

> /s/ Cody Rice-Velasquez

>  Cody Rice-Velasquez

>  Defendant, Pro Se

> 

> —

> CERTIFICATE OF SERVICE

> I certify that on this 9th day of March, 2026, a copy of the foregoing Motion for Sanctions Pursuant to Indiana Trial Rule 11 was served upon all parties of record through the Indiana E-Filing System.

> 

> Respectfully submitted,

> /s/ Cody Rice-Velasquez

> Cody Rice-Velasquez

> Defendant, Pro Se

> ```

> ```

### User
> non of my previews are showing for any file on my pc, what command do i put to remove the security protection blockign the previews.

### Assistant
> This happens in Windows when files are marked as **“downloaded from the internet” (Mark-of-the-Web)**. Windows blocks previews and sometimes editing.
> 
> You can remove it in **one PowerShell command**.
> 
> ## Run this in PowerShell
> 
> Navigate to the folder first, then run:
> 
> ```powershell
> Get-ChildItem -Recurse | Unblock-File
> ```
> 
> That will **remove the security block from every file in the folder and subfolders**, which usually restores previews immediately.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## If you want to target only PDFs (common for court docs)
> 
> ```powershell
> Get-ChildItem -Recurse *.pdf | Unblock-File
> ```
> 
> ---
> 
> ## If you want to unblock an entire drive folder like Downloads
> 
> ```powershell
> Get-ChildItem "C:\Users\$env:USERNAME\Downloads" -Recurse | Unblock-File
> ```
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Quick test for one file
> 
> Right-click file → **Properties** → bottom checkbox:
> 
> ```
> Unblock
> ```
> 
> If that box exists, it confirms the issue.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## One more thing (very common with court PDFs)
> 
> If previews **still don't show**, it can be because **Preview Handlers are disabled**.
> 
> Turn them back on:
> 
> 1. Open **File Explorer**
> 2. Click **View**
> 3. Enable **Preview Pane**
> 4. Then go to  
>    `View → Options → View`
> 5. Make sure this is **unchecked**:
> 
> ```
> Always show icons, never thumbnails
> ```
> 
> ---
> 
> If you want, I can also give you the **one registry tweak that permanently stops Windows from blocking downloaded files** (huge quality-of-life improvement when dealing with court filings and PDFs).
